# 046 - Introduction to Algebra ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 46 ｜ N J Wildberger [5c3WyrebRYI].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. This is a series on the foundations of mathematics where I attempt to set up a somewhat new and better foundation for the subject. It's a very ambitious program, and we've just begun the subject. We've talked about arithmetic and some sort of elementary aspects of how to set that up. We've talked about geometry and some elementary aspects of how to set that up. Now it's time to go to the third most important aspect of the subject. Mathematics has three key areas: arithmetic is the primary one; it's the most important and historically the oldest. Geometry is the second most important and the second oldest, and the third most important and the third oldest is algebra. So today we're going to initiate our discussion of algebra, and we're going to have quite a lot to say about algebra. It's a very rich subject, and I think a lot of what we're going to say also impacts primary school education and high school education too. As you will see, we'll also have a chance to see how to look at some very standard things in a new light, perhaps a better light. So let's start by positioning this subject roughly in terms of arithmetic and geometry. I think of algebra as forming a bridge between arithmetic and geometry. It emanates from arithmetic and ties these two fundamental disciplines together. If I had to say what algebra was in a single sentence, perhaps simplified, I would say something like algebra is generalized arithmetic. We take the arithmetic that we've been studying—just arithmetic with natural numbers, integers, fractions, rational numbers, the basic arithmetic involving the operations of addition, multiplication, subtraction, and division—and then we look to extend those operations and those numbers. We're going to enlarge our idea of objects that we can do arithmetic with, and that's basically how algebra comes about. Historically, its origins probably go back to the Babylonians, but the Greeks already had a pretty good idea of algebra in a geometrical context. As I've already discussed in the talks about Greek geometry, Euclid had a kind of geometrical algebra where algebraic expressions were interpreted and proven geometrically. Somewhat later, Nicomachus wrote an influential book called "Introductio Arithmetica," in which he emphasized the importance of arithmetic essentially aside from geometry, not necessarily built up from geometry. The greatest arithmetician of the Greek times was Diophantus, who lived 250 years after Christ and whose name gives us the subject of Diophantine equations, which are equations where we're looking for solutions purely in terms of integers or sometimes rational numbers. He wrote a book called "Arithmetica," actually 13 books together—I think some of them have been lost—but nevertheless he gives us a very thorough treatment of a lot of different problems, somewhat unrelated perhaps, and not a lot of systematic theory, but he's able to solve a lot of interesting problems, such as this one here: find three numbers such that their sum and the sum of any two of them is a square. But algebra as a separate discipline from arithmetic really only gets going with the Hindus and then later the Arabs. So let's have a look at that development. Earlier in this series, we've already remarked that Hindu mathematics was notable for their introduction of essentially our numerical system. The decimal number system that we use to represent numbers is basically a gift to us from the Hindu mathematicians and a crucially important one. So given their very strong foundation arithmetically, it's no surprise that they were able to solve a lot of reasonably sophisticated algebraic problems. Some names that are notable are Aryabhata, who lived around 400 AD, Brahmagupta around 600 AD, and Bhaskara in the 12th century. Aspects of this Hindu arithmetic algebra include, first of all, a richer numerical system which was quite comfortable with working with fractional, negative numbers, and also irrational numbers like square roots of natural numbers—square root of 3, square root of 5, and so on. They were very familiar with quadratic equations and how to solve a quadratic equation and more sophisticated things too. A very important number theoretical Diophantine equation, called Pell's equation nowadays, which goes back much earlier to even Archimedes, is x^2 - 61y^2 = 1. If you try to solve that equation for integers x and y, then aside from the sort of trivial solution 1, you'll be challenged to find a solution if you don't know some of the theory that the Hindu mathematicians and subsequent generations discovered. After the Hindus, the torch passed to the Arab mathematicians, who took the Hindu works, took the Greek works, studied them both, put them together, and developed them further. The name of algebra, in fact, comes from the work of Muhammad ibn Musa al-Khwarizmi (not sure if I'm pronouncing that right) around 800 AD, who wrote a book called in Arabic "Al-Kitab al-Mukhtasar fi Hisab al-Jabr wal-Muqabala," which roughly means "The Compendious Book on Calculation by Completion and Balancing." The kinds of things that he was referring to with "completion" is this process: say you have an equation x^2 - 3 = 13, where x is an unknown number that you're trying to determine. Then what you can do is you can take the -3 away from the left-hand side and you can restore it by essentially taking it away from the right-hand side or adding it to the right-hand side. This is a very typical kind of basic operation in algebra. Other mathematicians, like Omar Khayyam around 1100, who was notable for being a literary great as well (he wrote the Rubaiyat), were very interested in cubic equations and showed how to solve certain cubic equations using conic sections. Alright, so with that very brief historical introduction, what happens in algebra? Well, one of the key ideas certainly is that we're going to use letters instead of numbers, or letters together with numbers. This has a lot of advantages and it allows general laws to be more easily stated—something that we've already done so far. It often clarifies the essentials of a problem in that you get to see the wood, not just the trees. It leads to various higher arithmetics, especially related to things called polynomials, which are going to play an important role in algebra. And it shows the connections between different kinds of questions—that certain kinds of questions are actually very much the same. But a natural question that bedevils a lot of first-time learners of algebra is: what do these letters actually mean? What is x? What is y? And that's something that we're going to talk about. It turns out that it's not so simple to say what those things are because the nature of the x's and y's and z's and so on that appear in algebra changes as you become a little bit more sophisticated. When you start off with the subject, x, y, and z have a certain role and a certain meaning, but later on, that meaning changes in quite a different direction. And so there's a subtle but important shift that we're going to have to pay attention to because we're interested in what's really going on and being precise about the basic structures and the basic objects that we're going to be using. Okay, so we're going to roughly follow a program like this: to start off with, first we're going to talk about what we might call Baby Algebra, which is more or less arithmetic with letters. Then we're going to move on to polynomials, and I'm going to introduce things called poly numbers, which are very closely related to polynomials but have some interesting advantages. We're going to talk about rational functions, which are a very important field of doing arithmetic, and we're going to talk about matrices, which is another extension of arithmetic. And of course, algebra has much more in it, but probably this will be good for a first stab at what the subject is about. So next time we are going to start with Baby Algebra, the algebra that students learn when they're 10, 11, 12 years old. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music] 

---

# 047 - Baby Algebra ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 47 ｜ N J Wildberger [oN5kw9i7qTA].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger, and today we're going to start talking about the basics of algebra. We're going to start off with very simple algebra, the kind that students learn in public school. This kind of algebra involves unknown quantities which are denoted by letters, usually the letters at the end of the alphabet like x, y, and z. So for us now, x is an unknown number, and we're given an equation like this one: x plus two equals seven. It's a game. If you have a ten-year-old daughter like I do, you can ask her, "With x plus two equals 7, what is x?" She'll think for a little while and figure out that x equals 5. If I give her a slightly harder one like this, it might be a little bit more challenging: x plus 13 equals 41. But eventually, she might figure out that x equals 28. However, with a more complicated question like this involving bigger numbers: x plus 378 equals 642, how is she going to figure out that the answer is 264? Some kind of manipulation of the equation is necessary to reduce it to a simpler equation. It turns out that although the original equation involves addition, the actual solution of the problem uses subtraction. So if we start off with x plus 378 equals 642, the way we solve that is by rewriting that equation in this form: we bring the 378 on this side to the other side, where the plus sign turns into a minus sign. So that's how we can then calculate that x equals 264. These two equations are considered to be equivalent in the sense that if we have a solution to this one, then it's also a solution to this one. Conversely, if we have a solution to this one, then it's also a solution to this one. This is a basic strategy in algebra, even in more complicated algebra, to take an equation involving some unknowns and simplify it to get an equivalent equation which is easier or simpler to solve. Here are some other examples: y minus 5 equals 3, illustrating that the variable doesn't have to be x. It could be y or z or anything else. In this case, there's a minus 5 on the same side as the y, and so we like to bring the minus 5 to the other side where it becomes a plus five. So we get y equals three plus five, which of course is eight. How about this equation: z plus ten equals two? Well, if we bring the ten to the other side with the minus sign, we get z equals two minus ten. Now the answer is minus eight, and we see that even if we start with a problem involving only natural numbers, the solution might very well take us into the realm of the integers. So in general, for this kind of equation, we need to consider our numbers to include integers. Now let's have a look at some slightly more sophisticated problems. Suppose we have the equation 3x equals twelve, and our convention is—and we have to be clear about what the convention is—that 3x means three times x. You can see why we might use this convention because if we're using x as a variable, then it can easily get confused with our multiplication symbol. So when we write 3x, it means 3 times x, or similarly, 3y means 3 times y, and so on. Alright, well if 3 times x equals 12, then if you know your multiplication table, x equals 4 because 3 times 4 is 12. If 7x equals 49, then from your multiplication table, x equals 7. But if you have 19x equals 893, your multiplication table probably doesn't go up that far, so you don't just automatically know what the answer is. Although the question involves multiplication, the solution uses division. In other words, we take this equation 19x equals 893 and replace it with the equivalent equation obtained by dividing both sides by 19. If we divide this by 19, we just get simply x, and if we divide this by 19, well we get that fraction, and 893 divided by 19 turns out to be 47 once you do the long division. Here are some other examples in the same kind of spirit: 4y equals 36. Well, then y equals 36 over 4, and that's 9. Here's another example: 5z equals 11. Dividing by 5, we get z equals 11 over 5. But now, as opposed to the previous cases, 11 divided by 5 is not a natural number or an integer, so we must remain with this quantity as simply a fraction. This kind of question generally requires us to extend our number system to include rational numbers. Fractions or rational numbers—I recall that fractions for me are always positive and rational numbers can include negative fractions as well. So we can use both of those types of equations and put them together to get somewhat more complicated examples. For example, 3x plus 4 equals 11 minus 2x plus 8. That's a single equation in an unknown variable x. The assumption is that this variable x and this variable x represent the same mystery number, and we have to find out what that mystery number is. What we generally do is replace this equation with a series of equivalent equations obtained from this one by standard operations. Generally speaking, we try to get all the x's on one side and all the numbers on the other side. Then we try to simplify everything until we finally have x equals some number. So in this case, 3x plus 2x on this side becomes a plus 2x when we bring it over there. We have 11 plus 8. The 4 here becomes a minus 4 when we bring it to the other side. So 5x equals 15, and x equals 3. Another example involving some fractions involving our unknown variable x: 6x minus 1 divided by 2 equals 5 plus 3x divided by 7, and x becomes the fraction 17 over 36. Manipulation of these kinds of algebraic equations relies heavily on a prior understanding of arithmetic with fractions. If you don't know how to do arithmetic with fractions fluidly, then you're always going to struggle with algebra. This is a very important point. It's one reason why—get rid of those calculators when you're teaching students mathematics. Force them to work with pen and paper to become confident and skilled at arithmetic with fractions because it's the basis for moving to algebra. So here's a fun little party game that you're all probably familiar with. Let's look at a particular example. Suppose that we're asked to pick a number and say add three. We're going to think of our number as being x instead of 7 or 12 or whatever. Let's think of it being an x; this turns out to clarify what's going on. So after we've added three, we have x plus three. Suppose the next instruction is to multiply it by two. Well, two times x plus three is 2x plus six. The next instruction is to add ten. Alright, so then we have 2x plus 16. The next instruction might be to subtract 4. Well, then we have 2x plus 12. Then we are told to divide by 2. Well, if we divide 2x plus 12 by 2, what we're going to get is x, because 2x divided by 2 is x, plus 6, because 12 divided by 2 is 6. Okay, and then finally we're asked to subtract our original number. So there's the number we got right now; the original number was x. If we take x plus six and we subtract x, then we end up with six. And you can see that yes, the answer is six. It is independent of the number x that you started with. Next time, we're going to have a look at solving quadratic equations, which is a big step up from these linear equations we've been looking at so far, but it's still part of Baby Algebra. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.

# 048b - Solving a quadratic equation 48b ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations ｜ N J Wildberger [mrY0mr1kGc0].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Alright, so here's the general setup that explains what we're doing here. We're going to look not at this particular equation but at a more general equation which is just like it: x squared plus bx equals c. So there's more letters now. There's the letter x which represents our mystery unknown number, and now there's letters b and c which are not representing mystery numbers anymore; they're representing constant given numbers. It's just that we're not specific about which ones they are. So here and here, b was 10 and c was 39, but we want to explain the algorithm in a way that doesn't depend on which particular numbers we're using, so we use b and c. Okay, but these b and c letters are playing a different role from x. We imagine that b and c are known to us and we have to solve for x. So what do we do? Well, we mimic what we did up here. We add b over 2 squared to both sides—that's the little square that we're adding there—and now what's on the left-hand side is a perfect square. Just like this here is a perfect square. It is equal to x plus b over 2 all squared, and let's check that. If we square this, we get x squared plus 2 times the product 2 times x times b over 2—that's exactly bx—and b over 2 squared, well, there it is right there. And this thing here, we can just write it as c plus b squared over 4. Now in this form, it's much like this equation here. We take x plus b over 2 squared equals this, so x plus b over 2 will be the square root of this where we're introducing a new terminology—the idea of a square root, a number whose square is this. So we end up getting x equals the square root of c plus b squared over 4 minus b over 2 because the b over 2 comes around to the other side. We're introducing this third notation for the square root; that was an innovation also due to the Arabs. So for example, the square root of 16 is 4, the square root of 25 is 5, and so on. Alright, let's have a look at this example which mimics exactly what we're doing here but rewritten in a more modern form that's familiar to all students of algebra. So here is the modern way of rewriting what Al-Khwarizmi did in that previous example. Usually, today, we think about a quadratic equation in this form: ax squared plus bx plus c equals zero, where a, b, and c are three specific given numbers, and x is an unknown number that we're trying to find. We'll assume that this coefficient a is non-zero so we really have a quadratic equation. Now there's a step-by-step procedure to transform this equation into an equation for x, and this is a well-known formula that all students learn. How to get that solution is almost as important; perhaps maybe it's more important than the solution itself. So it's something that all serious students of mathematics should know—the various steps to go from here to here. So what do we do? The first thing is we take the a and we take it as a factor: a times x squared plus b over a times x, and we bring the c to the other side, giving us minus c there. We divide by a, so this a comes over to the other side as minus c over a, and what's inside here. Now we complete the square in exactly the same way as we did on the previous slide. We do that by adding the square of half of this coefficient b over 2a, so we add the square of that to both sides, so that it is still a valid equation. That makes what's on the left-hand side here a perfect square: (x plus b over 2a) all squared. When we simplify what's on the right, the common denominator is 4a squared, and there will be a b squared from here. When we take this over a common denominator of 4a squared, we have to multiply the top by 4a, giving us b squared minus 4ac in the numerator here. So this squared equals this quantity, so what's inside is the square root of the right-hand side. We put the square root of the numerator and the square root of the denominator, which will be 2a. Because the square root has an ambiguity in terms of sign, if we have one solution, the negative of it is also a solution. We put a plus or minus here, telling us that we can either take plus this or minus this. The square root is by convention always a positive number, so when we talk about the square root of 9, we always mean 3, not minus 3. To go from here to here, it's simply a question of taking this b over 2a and bringing it to the other side. So finally, x equals minus b plus or minus the square root of b squared minus 4ac all over 2a. This is a well-known formula for all students, and in our context, we can actually use this as long as this condition is satisfied: that what's inside here, this b squared minus 4ac term, had better be a square because we want to take the square root of it. So in particular, it's going to be positive; it's got to be positive and it's got to be a square because right now we're still working in the realm entirely of rational numbers. Okay, so that's good. That's the classic formula for the quadratic equation. Next time, we're going to talk about an important ingredient to this business, which is: how do you actually find a square root? We've been assuming that you can kind of do it, but how do you actually find the square root of a number? That's a very interesting algorithm which we'll discuss next time. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.

# 048 - Solving a quadratic equation ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 48a ｜ N J Wildberger [7eZUzceFFJw].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. We are now starting to talk about algebra in this series on foundational aspects of mathematics, and today we're going to talk about a rather important step up from linear equations: quadratic equations, which have a very long and illustrious history. So the basic problem is something like this: suppose that we have the equation x^2 = 49. What does x^2 mean? It means x * x, okay? That's called the squaring operation. So we'd like to know what is the solution to this equation. What can x be? Well, if you know your multiplication table, you'll know that 7 * 7 is 49, so there's the solution x = 7. But if you think about it for a little while, you'll also realize that -7 is also a solution because -7 * -7 is also 49. So if we're going to allow negative numbers—and we don't have to—but if we are going to, then there's actually two solutions: x = 7 and x = -7. Now for this kind of quadratic equation, knowing what the squares are is very useful. This is something that probably most public school students haven't seen, but it's a good thing for them to see. They don't have to memorize it, but they should have at least seen this table of squares up to a certain amount. So here I've shown you what they all are: probably from 1 up to 100. It's familiar, maybe 11 squared, 12 squared, familiar. But then 13 squared is 169, 14 squared is 196, 15 squared is 225, 16 squared is 256, 17 squared is 289, 18 squared is 324, 19 squared is 361, 20 squared is 400, 100 squared is 10,000. Those are all useful values to know. But what if we're given a bigger equation like x^2 = 6,084? How did I get 6,084? Well, I took the number 78 and I squared it. So this is an easy way of generating a quadratic equation: you take some number, you square it, and then you give your friend this equation. Say, "What is x^2 equals blah blah blah?" You know because you've taken a number and squared it, but for him to unravel that operation might be a little bit tricky. So we're going to try to think about this kind of quadratic equation and some more general variants of it. But before we do, let's have a look at some general laws which are very pertinent to understanding quadratic equations. The first is what happens when we square x + 1: (x + 1)^2. So that's (x + 1) * (x + 1). If we use the distributive law here, there's an x here and another x here for a total of 2x, and then there's also a +1. So a very important equation or identity is that (x + 1)^2 is x^2 + 2x + 1. This is important because, first of all, it allows us to generate this table a little bit more efficiently than squaring each individual number. For example, suppose we know that 20^2 is 400 and we want to know what is 21 squared. Well, according to this little rule, it's 20^2 + 2 * 20 which is 40 + 1. So to go from here to here, we just take this one, add two times the value here which is 20, and add one. Similarly, to go from here to here, we take this number, add two times that, which would be 42. We add that, and we get 483, and then we add one more to get 484. That's a somewhat faster way of generating a table like this. This identity has an important generalization when we replace the one with a general y: (x + y)^2. All students of algebra learn that this is x^2 + 2xy + y^2. Here we're already seeing a kind of change in our use of the variables x and y. When I write down this equation, I'm no longer thinking of x and y as unknown quantities. I'm rather thinking of them as representing arbitrary or general numbers. So it's no longer a question of having a look at this and saying what is x, what is y. x and y are not mystery numbers. This is a general law that applies to any x and any y. It's a slightly different usage of the letters x and y. We'll keep that in mind for later. So here's a more general quadratic equation: x^2 + 10x = 39. x is some mystery number that satisfies this equation, and I want you to tell me what x is. Well, you can try trial and error, try x = 1, try x = 2, and so on until eventually you'll get it. In this case, it's not too hard, but a general strategy for solving this kind of equation has been known by many cultures. So the ancient Chinese, the Babylonians, the Arabs, the Hindus—they all had ideas of how to solve this kind of equation. In particular, Al-Khwarizmi, who we mentioned before around 800 AD, had the following rather nice interpretation. So we think of an L-shaped figure like this, where this has side x (whatever x is) and this has side five. Here, symmetrically, x and five. We're thinking now about the area of this L-shaped region. There's an x^2 here for that square, and each of these rectangles has area 5x. So altogether, the area is x^2 + 10x because we have two of these rectangles. That's exactly the left-hand side. Now what we do is we think about completing this diagram to a square. In other words, these dotted lines here—we add another portion so that now we have a complete square. Once we've done that, we have a square whose side is x + 5. So this total area here will be (x + 5)^2, and that's going to be the area that we started with, which we're assuming is 39, plus the area of the square that we've added. What is the area of the square that we've added? Well, it's 5 by 5, so this area here is 25. So (x + 5)^2, the entire area here, is 39 + 25, which we work out is 64. Now the equation has been transformed from an equation like this to the equation (x + 5)^2 = 64. This is very much like the simpler kind of equation that we've already been talking about. Its solution is x + 5 = 8 because 8 * 8 is 64. From that, we can deduce that x = 8 - 5, which is three. Now there's another solution too because we could also have chosen -8 as the square root of 64. So there would be another solution here obtained by taking -8 - 5, which is -13. 

---

# 049 - How to find a square root ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 49 ｜ N J Wildberger [EnxV3_1oaOU].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger, and today we're going to look at the algorithm of finding the square root of a number. This is an important operation that goes back to antiquity—to the ancient Greeks, to the Chinese, to the Arabs, Babylonians, and even to the Hindus. It's important because it's the basis for solving quadratic equations. Without being able to take a square root, the quadratic formula doesn't make too much sense. Let's start off with an example and show you three different approaches to the subject. The one that we're mainly interested in is one developed by the Hindu mathematicians, and it's a very practical algorithm that you can try yourself after you've learned it. Alright, so here's an example: 523 squared equals this number, 2703529. We say that 523 is the square root of this number. The idea is we are given this number to start with, and we're trying to find the square root. One way of approaching it, which is a good way for public school students to start thinking about the subject, is to make some guesses. 500 squared is 250,000, while 600 squared is 360,000, so the real answer must lie somewhere in between those two guesses. Then we can make some further guesses, keep on squaring the guesses that we have, and see if we're getting bigger or less than our starting number, and eventually we'll zero in on our number 523. Here's another method that goes back to the ancient Babylonians. Basically, the idea is this: suppose that x squared equals n is the equation we're trying to solve. So what we do is we make an initial guess x1, we compute n divided by x1, then we set x2 to be the average of these two numbers—the average of x1 and n divided by x1—and then we repeat the process using x2 instead of x1. The idea is that if x1 is exactly the square root, then n divided by x1 will be equal to x1, and these two numbers will be equal. Otherwise, one of them will be less than and one of them will be bigger than the square root, and so averaging those two numbers, it's a relatively good guess for the square root. If we keep doing that, then we approach the square root value. This method is essentially the same as Newton's method, discovered by Isaac Newton, which is taught in calculus courses. Without going into too much detail, the idea is roughly that we have a curve and we want to find its zero. Say the curve is represented by the equation x squared minus n, then what we do is we take one guess here and find the tangent, see where that tangent intersects the x-axis, and then that's our next guess. Then the next tangent gives us the next guess, and so on. It turns out that this Babylonian method, four thousand years ago, is essentially Newton's method in this particular case. However, for pen-and-paper calculations, or even by computer, it's a little bit slow in the sense that you have to rely on dividing and repeated divisions. So let's have a look at the third method, which is due to the Hindus. This Vedic method relies on the following identity: (x + y) squared is x squared plus we can write the rest in this form: 2x + y times y. The basic idea is that we're going to have an initial approximation, and then we're going to guess how much we have to adjust that initial approximation by computing a term like this. So in our example of 523 squared, let's make the following observations: 500 squared is 250,000, okay, so that's less than our number n. 520 squared will be, according to this law here, it's 500 squared plus 2 times 500 plus 20, so 1,000 plus 20 times 20. It's a way of thinking of how you go from here to here. What you're doing is you're adding this new component, and that turns out to be this number here. Then the next stage, 523 all squared, well that's what we have at the previous stage, that's 520 squared right there, plus something extra. The extra term given by 2 times 500 and 20 plus 3 times 3. This is a clue to how to set up the Vedic algorithm. Here's how it works: We write down the number that we're trying to find the square root of, and we divide it into groups of two. We look at the leftmost group of two and we find its square root, which is a number between 0 and 9. In this case, 27 is close; the square root is 5. 5 squared is 25, and if we subtract 25 from here, the 2 comes down and then we also bring down the next pair of numbers. Now we have 235. This corresponds to our initial guess of 500 squared. In the next step, what we have to do is we are going to multiply what we have up here, the 5, by 2. 5 times 2 is 10, and we're going to leave a little space for an extra digit. That's like that y, and we're going to try to find an extra digit here, in this case 2, which has the property that this digit times 10 with a digit is pretty close to our remainder, 235. So we're looking for a digit number between 0 and 9 so that when you add it to 10, 1 times digit and multiply it by the digit y, then you are getting something that's close but less than 235. If we tried 1, for example, we would have 101 times 1, well that would be less than 235. But 2 also works, 102 times 2 is 204, which is less than 235. 3, however, would be too big because 103 times 3 would be bigger than 235. So 2 is the right number, and we subtract 204 and we get 31. Then we pull the 29 down, and then we're going to repeat the same process with 329. What we do is we take 52, which we've already got, multiply it by 2, there's 104, and we add room for an extra digit. Now we have to choose that digit so that when we multiply the digit 2 times this number, we get something that's less than or equal to our remainder. It turns out that 3 exactly works, and when we subtract, we get a remainder of 0, telling us that 523 is the square root of our original number. So let's look at another example. Alright, so here's the number that we're trying to find the square root of. We write that number down and we group its digits into groups of 2. Looking at the leftmost group, its square root or closest to it will be 7. 7 squared is 49, we subtract and we get 22. Then we pull down the next pair of digits for a remainder of 263. Now we're going to take the seven, multiply it by 2 for 14, and try to find the new digit, so y, so that y times 14y is close to 263 but less than it. Well, 1 is the best one because if we use 2, we get 142 times 2; that would be bigger than this. So we put a 1 there, 1 times 141, we subtract, we get 122, and then we pull down the 85. At this stage, we have 71 already. Multiply 71 by 2 right there, now we have to guess what the new digit is so that this times this is less than our remainder. Well, you have to adjust, but there are only possibilities from 1 to 9, so it's not that bad. You guess 8, and 8 works. 8 times this is this number here. Take the subtraction and you get 861. Finally, we pull down the last pair of numbers, 96. Take 718, multiply it by 2, right there, and leave a space. Find a digit, 6 in this case, so that's 6 times this number with a 6 there, is less than or equal to our number or remainder. Now it works out exactly, so we get 0, meaning we've got an exact square root. The answer is 7186. Next time, we're going to have a look at how algebra helps us understand patterns generated by special numbers. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.

# 050 - Algebra and number patterns ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 50 ｜ N J Wildberger [0QwESq4hCZQ].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. In this series on foundational issues in mathematics, we've been talking about algebra. Algebra is a large subject characterized by the use of letters to often represent numbers, and we've talked about the usage of letters in equations as representing unknown numbers. Today, we're going to look at a different role for letters in algebra by first of all having a look at some interesting patterns formed from geometrical sequences. Here are the squares. These are actually literally geometrical squares, and we're counting the areas of these squares: 1² = 1, 2² = 4, 3² = 9, and so on. These numbers are important numbers in mathematics; they're called the squares, and we can represent them algebraically by the use of the term n². So the nth square is n², which I remind you means n * n. Here the letter n is representing a natural number, in other words, 1, 2, 3, 4, and so on. Here's another famous family of special numbers: the triangular numbers. Let's give them a name; let's call the nth triangular number T_n. Geometrically, how they arise is by stacking up some balls in a triangular kind of pyramid: there's one ball, there's three, there's six, there's ten, there's fifteen, and we can continue. At each stage, what we're doing to go from here to here, say, is we're adding an extra row of balls at the bottom so that T₃ there is reproduced here, and then we add four more balls to get T₄. Similarly, the T₄ is reproduced here, and we're adding five new balls to get T₅. Here are the triangular numbers: 1, 3, 6, 10, and 15, and so on. It's a little bit less obvious now what a general formula for the nth triangular number is, and that's what we want—something just like this but telling us what exactly that Tₙ is in terms of n. Triangular numbers have been studied since antiquity, and they have lots of interesting properties. Here is a way of analyzing patterns of numbers in a more general way which applies also to other interesting sequences or patterns. We write down the sequence starting, say, with 1, 3, 6, 10—these are our triangular numbers—and then above any two of them we write what the difference is. The difference between 3 and 1 is 2, the difference between 6 and 3 is 3, and so on. Or to put it another way, we write how much we have to add to this one to get to the next one, and we've seen already that these differences increase linearly. They go through all the natural numbers. That's made a little bit clearer by actually deciding to start our triangular numbers with zero. Then we have a difference of one as well, which makes it aesthetically a little bit more pleasant. So let's do that. We could also examine this sequence, which is just the sequence of all natural numbers in terms of differences. Well, the difference between any two of them is just one, so there's the line of differences between the natural numbers, and of course, the differences between these are all zero, and that's sort of where things stop. What about a formula for the nth triangular number? Well, here's T₄, and another way of thinking about what that is is it's 1 + 2 + 3 + 4. So there it is there as a sum. Another way of representing this triangle of circles is to represent it more with some boxes: 4, 3, 2, 1. If we do it that way, then we can play the following trick. We can take our copy of T₄ over here in blue, and then we can take a second copy of it as well in red, invert it, and put it on top to match up and form a rectangle. In this case, a rectangle of sides four and five. That's telling us that 2 * T₄ is 4 * 5, and that works in general. 2 * Tₙ is going to be a rectangle with sides n and n + 1, so the total area will be n * (n + 1), from which we deduce that Tₙ is equal to n * (n + 1) / 2. So here's another use of letters in algebra to allow us to write formulas for patterns. The n here is not representing an unknown number so much as a general number, and it's allowing us to express an arithmetical fact that's valid not just for one triangular number but for any triangular number. In fact, this formula is an important formula because it's directly connected with this kind of arithmetic sequence. In other words, we can interpret that little computation we made about Tₙ as a formula for this arithmetic series: 1 + 2 + 3 + all the way up to n. The sum of all the numbers from 1 to n, which is just the number of balls in the nth triangular number, is n * (n + 1) / 2. That's our formula for Tₙ. This kind of formula is sometimes a little bit ambiguous, and so we mathematicians like to introduce a more precise notation for talking about sums. This is the summation notation, represented by this capital Greek letter Sigma (Σ) for sum. So here's our convention: instead of writing 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 + 5, we'll simply write the sum from k = 1 to 5 of k. So this expression right here means this by definition. Here's a more general example: the sum of all the numbers from 1 to n, 1 + 2 + 3 + 4 all the way up to n - 1 and up to n. That sum is now represented in the summation notation by this sign, the sigma. That stands for sum. This index k is telling us what, first of all, we're summing and what the range of summation is. k is running from 1 to n, and we're summing k. The beauty of this summation notation is that it's quite flexible and allows us to talk about other kinds of sums. For example, 1² + 2² + 3² + 4² + 5² could be written more economically as the sum k = 1 to 5 of k². Here's the general formula that extends this. I'll just give it to you for now; we'll have a look at the proof later on. The sum of all the squares from 1 up to n, so the sum k = 1 to n of k² is n * (n + 1) * (2n + 1) / 6. So if n happened to be five, then we would have a five here, a six here, an eleven here, the sixes would cancel, and we would get 55. This last sum that we looked at, the sum of all the squares, can also be studied in terms of a table of differences. It's also quite interesting. The differences between the squares are all the odd numbers: 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, and so on. Further differences are all two and then zero, so that's relatively easy. But we can also extend this table downwards as long as we have a starting point. So suppose we start with zero. Then this line is going to be the line of the sums of squares. This 55 here represents 1² + 2² + all the way up to 5². That's the number we already calculated. So here are the sums of squares. That's a more complicated sequence from the sequence of squares obtained by going down in this table of differences. This is just the beginning of talking about patterns of numbers, and next time we're going to look at some more patterns to give us some more practice in trying to understand geometrical patterns arithmetically and using algebra to encode the results. So I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music]

So for example, if we start with 1, then this would have to be 4 so that 1 plus 3 is 4. So we had a new sequence of numbers, which might be called the tetrahedral numbers. In this sequence, the 20 represents the sum 1 plus 3 plus 6 plus 10. So each one of these numbers is the sum of the first n triangular numbers. Here's the geometrical interpretation of the tetrahedral numbers. Something like 20 can be thought of in terms of a three-dimensional stack of triangular numbers. So there are six on top of them, and then we could furthermore stack another three on top of those, and then one on the top, giving us a nice little tetrahedral pyramid. The whole number in this particular pyramidal array would be 1 plus 4 plus 9 plus 16, which is 30. It's not at all so obvious how to define pentagonal numbers, especially since fitting cannonballs around layers of five doesn't work very well, but here's one natural approach that's been studied for a long time. We start with a pentagon with one cannonball right in the middle, and then we enlarge that picture—so we'll put that one right there—by adding another layer, making a bigger pentagon around the original one. Then there are three more sides with two on each side, so we're adding four to get a total of five. Then in the next step, we copy this picture over here, and then we add another layer, or as maybe the ancient Greeks would call such a thing, an "omen," another layer of cannonballs, this time three on each side of three sides, getting a total of twelve. We can continue this; at each stage, we add another layer. Now this layer has four, four, four in each of the three rows that we're adding to the original. In this way, we get a sequence of numbers which we might call pentagonal numbers: 1, 5, 12, 22, and it's a nice exercise to try to convince yourself that the formula for P_n is this thing here: n times (3n - 1) over 2. So now it's clear how to extend this table. We could carry on this way without actually drawing pictures, just by extending the table of differences. This would allow us to carry on. We can also extend, as we did before, to higher-dimensional analogs. In this case, it's not so clear what it means. Well, if we do, there's only really one reasonable way of doing it because this pattern of threes obviously wants to continue. So we have to continue this pattern of threes. Well, if this is a three and that's four, that we know, then this has got to be one because this plus this has got to be this. Then this has to be minus 2 because this plus three has to equal one—that's forced. And then that's forced, and that's forced. Now we're getting going down by three here. Now what happens to the pentagonal numbers themselves when we extend them? Here there's a one, here there's one, there must be a zero here, and two plus minus two is zero. That's got to be two, that's got to be seven in order for seven plus minus five to equal two. That's got to be fifteen, that's got to be twenty-six, and now it seems as if we're getting another second series of pentagonal numbers which are not, at least at first, recognizable in a geometrical form. # 052 - Leonhard Euler and Pentagonal numbers ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 52 ｜ N J Wildberger [1mSk3J3GlA8].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger, and today we're going to talk about Leonard Euler and pentagonal numbers. Euler was the greatest mathematician of the modern age. Originally Swiss, he spent a lot of his life in Germany and in Russia, in St. Petersburg. He lived in the time 1707 to 1783, during which he contributed to almost all branches of mathematics and established foundational notation, many important theorems, and in fact, discovered entire new branches of mathematics. Today we're going to look at how he interpreted the pentagonal numbers that we talked about in the previous video and connected them in a remarkable way with number theory. So I remind you that the pentagonal numbers are the sequence here, and by looking at the table of differences for these, we were able to extend these pentagonal numbers also to sort of negative values. The formula for the pentagonal numbers was n times (3n - 1) over 2, and last time I posed the question of whether these numbers over here, sort of fictitious pentagonal numbers, play an important role. Euler showed that they did. He considered putting the proper pentagonal numbers together with these other ones into a single sequence, and he observed that the differences formed by this sequence were rather irregular and did not have an easily understood pattern. Even back then, in the 18th century, Euler was aware of the importance of looking at a table of differences. What I've done here is I've just circled in green the proper pentagonal numbers that actually come from geometrical figures. Now let's take a little diversion to a little bit of elementary number theory, and I would explain to you an important function of a natural number called Sigma(n), which is the sum of the divisors of n. So a divisor of a natural number like 6 is another natural number that divides it. Six has divisors 1, 2, 3, and 6 itself, and Sigma of 6 is defined to be the sum of all these divisors, which in this case is 12. Here's a table of the values of the Sigma function for a natural number n. It's a rather interesting pattern which doesn't have a clear structure. We can see the primes reflected by the fact that if you take a prime like 7, then its Sigma will be 1 more than it because it only has two factors: 1 and itself. Also, 6 has the rather remarkable property that its Sigma is exactly 2 times it. That's what is known since ancient times as a perfect number. Alright, so there's n, there's Sigma(n), and a natural question is how could you calculate this? How could you extend this table without actually factoring a number and counting up the sum of all of its divisors? Euler discovered a beautiful way of doing just that. Euler discovered a recursive formula for the Sigma function, which means that the values for any particular n can be expressed in terms of values for smaller natural numbers. Here's the relation that he found: Sigma of capital N could be written as a sum of Sigmas of smaller numbers, explicitly Sigma of (n - 1) plus Sigma of (n - 2) minus Sigma of (n - 5) minus Sigma of (n - 7), and so on. What are these numbers that are appearing here that we're subtracting from n? They are exactly the generalized pentagonal numbers in the sequence we wrote down on the previous slide: 1, 2, 5, 7, 12, 15, 22, 26, and so on. There's also the issue of sign—some of them are plus and some of them are minus. Well, the rule that Euler stated was that if you look at the pentagonal number that you're considering subtracting, that's called P_n. If that n is an odd number, then Euler says we put a plus sign in front, and if n is an even number, we put a negative sign in front. In the sum, there are only a finite number of terms because we agree that the Sigma function is only defined for terms with positive values. So once what's inside here becomes negative, we ignore it. There's one more convention that if we end up with a Sigma of 0 as the last term, we must interpret Sigma of 0 as equaling n, the number that we started with. Alright, so let's illustrate by calculating Sigma of 12. So we subtract 1, so there's Sigma of 11. We subtract 2, Sigma of 10. We subtract 5 with a minus sign. We subtract 7 with a minus sign. We subtract 12 with a plus sign. Twelve minus twelve is zero, so this Sigma of 0 has to be interpreted as the number 12. These ones here we can look up in the table if we have all the values up to 11. Sigma of 11 is 12. Sigma of 10 is 18. Sigma of 7 is 8. Sigma of 5 is 6. So the formula says that Sigma of 12 is the sum of these things, which is 28, and that is indeed correct. This Sigma function is, in fact, a very interesting and important number theoretical function, and I can't resist throwing in a fascinating aside discovered relatively recently by Professor Jeffrey Lagarias from the University of Michigan in 2002. I've mentioned the Riemann hypothesis in an earlier video. I didn't tell you what it was; it's rather complicated if you set it out in the traditional way. So here is his equivalent formulation of the Riemann hypothesis. It's essentially that this inequality is true for any natural number. What does it say? It says that Sigma(n), the sum of the divisors of n, is less than or equal to H_n plus log of H_n times e to the H_n. What is H_n? It's this rational number here: the sum of all the fractions 1 + 1/2 + 1/3 all the way up to 1/n. Here's another discovery that Euler made, having to do with pentagonal numbers. It concerns another function called the partition function, which plays an important role in combinatorics. Alright, so without going into a lot of detail, let me just say rather quickly that P(n) is the number of ways to partition a natural number n. By that I mean, here's an example: P(4) is 5 because if we partition four, group four into blocks, each block consisting of a natural number, then here are the ways we can do it. We can write it as 4, or as 3 + 1, or as 2 + 2, or as 2 + 1 + 1, or as 1 + 1 + 1 + 1, and those are the five ways of partitioning 4. So we say that P(4) is 5. This partition function is an important function in computer science and other things, and it's also rather hard to compute. Euler discovered, again, a remarkable recursive formula for the partition function, and it turns out that it's essentially the exact same recursive formula that holds for the function Sigma. So for example, P(14)—to evaluate that, you take 14 minus 1, 14 minus 2, 14 minus 5, 14 minus 7, 14 minus 12. You put plus signs and minus signs in front just as we did before, and then you evaluate these ones which you assume you already know. There's what they happen to be, and if we calculate that, we get 135, telling us that the next term would be 135 without actually having to partition 14 into all possible ways. This becomes a much more efficient way of calculating the partition function, and it's still to this day probably one of the best ways of calculating the partition function. Next time I want to talk about another aspect of baby algebra, which is very important for public school and high school mathematics: the issue of identities. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music]

# 053 - Algebraic identities ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 53 ｜ N J Wildberger [cP50PQ-7VEk].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger, and today we're going to look at how letters arise in algebra in the context of identities. We've had already our share of identities when we were talking about the foundations of arithmetic, the basic laws satisfied by arithmetic with, say, rational numbers. For example, we observe that m plus n equals n plus m for any natural numbers m and n. So this one single expression captures at once a whole range of possible formulas valid for particular numbers. Special cases of this are, for example, 1 plus 2 equals 2 plus 1, or 2 plus 5 equals 5 plus 2, or 13 plus 24 equals 24 plus 13, or trillions of other examples you could come up with. All of these are special cases of this one law. We also framed some other laws: the commutative law for multiplication, the distributive law, the associative law for addition, and the associative law for multiplication. In each case, the m's, k's, and n's represent arbitrary numbers of a particular kind. So an algebraic identity encodes a law which is valid for generally any values assigned to the variables. This is a rule that's valid no matter which natural numbers we substitute for m, k, and n. That's an important usage of letters in algebra. Now there are many very rich identities in mathematics, starting from relatively simple ones to progressively more complicated ones to very rich and sophisticated identities. Let's start off by looking at some fundamental identities that are important for almost all students of mathematics. Next to the basic laws of arithmetic that we've already talked about, I think the simple quadratic identities are the next in line. We're talking about what (a + b) squared is. So that's (a + b) times (a + b), and all students learn that it's a squared plus 2ab plus b squared. So this is an identity that allows us to replace this with this or conversely this with this whenever we find it in an expression. A simple variant is (a - b) squared. It's almost the same; it's a squared minus 2ab plus b squared. And of course, we can verify these identities by simply using the distributive law to expand (a - b) times (a - b), for example. Another important quadratic identity is the difference of squares: (a + b) times (a - b) equals a squared minus b squared. Next in line, I suppose in terms of simplicity and importance, are the geometric series identities. The first one is really just a special case of this one: the fact that (1 + r) times (1 - r) equals 1 - r squared. Now you might ask why am I switching from a's and b's to r? Well, that's because in traditional mathematics, certain kinds of identities have certain variables associated with them, and so I'm trying to use the notation that's most commonly associated with the identities that we're talking about. A variant on this, slightly more complicated, is (1 + r + r squared) times (1 - r). It's important to verify that when you multiply this all out and collect terms, you get 1 - r cubed because here you have 1 + r + r squared and here you have -r - r squared - r cubed. So the r squared terms cancel; you're just left with 1 - r cubed. That kind of telescoping or collapsing of intermediate terms happens from larger expressions. If we take (1 + r + r squared + r cubed) and multiply by (1 - r), again the only thing that's left after you expand and cancel is 1 - r to the fourth. Then there's an obvious pattern that's established here, and we can write the general law involving not just a general letter r representing a number but also in terms of a general exponent n, generalizing the 2, 3, 4, and so on that appeared here. So in this context, the n represents a natural number, 1 in this sequence, while the r is potentially a rational number. This very important formula is the geometric series formula: 1 + r + r squared all the way up to r to the n is 1 - r to the n + 1 divided by 1 - r. It's what we get when we take an expression like this and divide by the 1 - r. That would be the case when n is 3, and because we're dividing by 1 - r, it's important to remember that the formula is only valid if this denominator is not 0, so we have to require that r is not equal to 1. The geometric series formula is often written like this, slightly more general, where the first term is a instead of one, but each term after that is obtained by multiplying by r. So the sum a + ar all the way up to ar to the n is a times 1 - r to the n + 1 divided by 1 - r. Here are two special identities that play a particularly important role in geometry. The first one we could call the Pythagorean identity, and here's the simple form: (1 - h squared) all squared plus (2h) all squared equals (1 + h squared) squared. I've decided to use h instead of some other variable for a pretty good reason. A generalization of this is m squared minus (n squared all squared) plus (2mn) all squared equals (m squared + n squared) all squared. All students of mathematics should verify this formula by explicitly expanding both sides and showing that they are in fact the same. The connection of this formula with Pythagorean triples is well known. For any values of m and n, natural numbers, we're getting three numbers here which have the property that this squared plus this squared equals this squared, giving us the sides of a right triangle according to Pythagoras' theorem. So for example, if m is 2 and n is 1, then these three numbers inside the brackets become 3, 4, and 5. 3 squared plus 4 squared equals 5 squared. You can generate other Pythagorean triples simply by changing the values of m and n. Here are two other important identities that are almost the same: Fibonacci's identities. This quantity squared plus this quantity squared equals this times this, and a slight variant: this same squared plus this thing squared equals again the same product of things. These two identities are crucial for trigonometry and for complex numbers. These are also important identities that all students of maths should verify by expansion. Next time we're going to talk about probably the most common and useful of all identities: the binomial theorem, which is a centerpiece of high school mathematics. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music]

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today we're going to start to establish proper, solid foundations for algebra. Such an approach, even though it may be useful and popular, is nevertheless weak logically, and we need to move up a step or several steps and address the issue of what exactly are the fundamental objects. The fundamental objects in algebra are closely related to polynomials, and we can focus our attention, to begin with, on this question of what exactly is a polynomial. Well, we've seen several subtly different ways in which this notion has manifested itself so far. We've talked about arithmetical expressions involving unknown numbers, for example, 3 + 2x^2 - x^3, where x represents an unknown number. We can think about formulas involving a general number n, for example, our formula for the nth triangular number. Sometimes a polynomial is considered as a function with a graph, something like this. Or we can think of a polynomial occasionally in terms of a constituent of an identity. For example, here's a variant on the geometric series identity. It's a very nice identity, and we can think, well, this is a polynomial here, this is a polynomial here, there are polynomials involved in identities like this. So there are a number of different possible ways of approaching polynomials, but it's absolutely necessary to decide on one and to specify and clarify completely what exactly this is. Most algebra texts fall short when it comes to this important task. But if we do not specify very clearly what the fundamental objects are, the entire theory that we build is logically weak and ultimately doesn't really make mathematical sense, even though it may give us a warm and fuzzy feeling. So let us turn now to the question: how are we going to think about what a polynomial is? My approach is rather unorthodox here. I believe that polynomials are properly built on a foundation of other objects called poly numbers, and I'm going to tell you what poly numbers are and how to do arithmetic with them. Once we understand that, then what a polynomial is will become very simple and elegant. So let's turn to the issue: what is a poly number? We're going to introduce things called poly numbers or, more particularly, positive poly numbers. We're going to introduce integral poly numbers, fractional poly numbers, and then ultimately rational poly numbers. It will turn out that rational poly numbers are also a field, just in the same way that the rational numbers are a field. Furthermore, these two fields—the field of rational numbers and the field of rational poly numbers—are the two most important fields in mathematics. That's number one and that's number two. So this is a very important setup that we're doing here. We have to understand these poly numbers, and we're going to proceed in a way which is very analogous to how we proceeded with setting up arithmetic in the first place. We're going to start with the poly equivalent of natural numbers. So a positive number is, by definition, a natural number or zero. We'll call that new type num plus. It's just a little bit more than the natural numbers; we've thrown in zero. When I started this video, I had to decide whether I was going to start with this as the basic object or this as the basic object, and there were good reasons to do either of them. In computer science, it's often useful to start counting with zero, but I felt that historically and perhaps intuitively it was more natural to start with one. However, it's clear that zero together with the natural numbers is also an important mathematical object, and so now we have a name for it and a new type. Alright, so now let's define what a positive poly number is. This is a fundamental definition: a positive poly number is a list of positive numbers written vertically with a bracket on top. So there's P; that's a positive poly number. n0, n1, n2 down to some nk—these are all positive numbers in the sense that we've just defined here. So they're all either zeros or natural numbers, and there's a bracket on top of this list, alerting us to the fact that this object is a new kind of object called a positive poly number. These are going to be the building blocks for this new arithmetic. So for example, here is a positive poly number: it's (1,3) with a bracket on top. Here is Q, which is (2,2,1,4). Here is R, which is just (1,7) with a bracket on top of it. Here is T, which is (0,3,0) with a bracket on top of it. Now there's an important notion of equality between two positive poly numbers. We say that P and P'—two positive poly numbers—are equal precisely when one can be obtained from the other by adding or removing zeros from the bottom. So for example, this positive poly number and this positive poly number are equal because this one's obtained from this one by adding a zero on the bottom or conversely this one's obtained from this one by removing a zero from the bottom. Here in this example, here are two positive poly numbers which are not equal: we've removed a zero, but we haven't removed it from the bottom, so that's no good. You're not allowed to do that. This is not equal to this. There are two basic operations on positive poly numbers, just as there are on natural numbers. These operations are addition and multiplication. Addition is very easy; multiplication is less easy. Let me illustrate these by giving you a challenge. I'm going to just illustrate what the two operations do in some examples. I'm going to ask you to go away and figure out what the operations are by studying these examples. I'm only going to give you one example for addition because it's so easy. So here's an example of the addition of this poly number with this poly number: (1,2,3) + (2,3,1) = (3,5,4). I've given you three examples of multiplication: (1,2) * (2,1) = (2,5,2); (1,1,1) * (1,1,1) = (1,2,3,2,1); (2,0,1) * (1,1) = (2,2,1). I want you to study these three examples. That's multiplication of two polynomials, and it satisfies some various laws: the commutative law, the associative law, a distributive law with respect to addition. And note that I'm using the usual convention that multiplication takes priority over addition, so you have to multiply these two first, you multiply these two, and then you add these two resulting. Now, we know that we don't usually leave it in this form because we don't like to have numbers bigger than nine in any one spot, so we would convert this 11 tens into 1 hundred and 10 tens, giving us altogether 416. # 059 - More arithmetic with polynumbers ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 59 ｜ N J Wildberger [KbNYsNWRtzs].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. In the last few videos, we've been talking about positive poly numbers, or we might just say poly numbers for short. These are fundamental building blocks for algebra, and the way I want you to think about them is that they are extensions of ordinary numbers or more sophisticated versions of numbers. If you look at the way we add two positive poly numbers or the way we multiply them, if you just look at the top level, the zero-degree terms, then you just have ordinary addition of numbers or ordinary multiplication of numbers. So at the top level, at degree zero, we are just getting ordinary arithmetic, and what happens below is some kind of sophisticated or more fancy arithmetic that's built up from it. Today let's start off by talking about another operation: the idea of multiplying a polynomial to give us another poly number. For example, here is a poly number: (2, 3) multiplied by the ordinary number 5 gives us the poly number (10, 15). The rule is simply that you take a poly number, multiply it by m, a number, and you get the new poly number whose entries are multiplied by m. So we're scaling the poly number, and this operation has some obvious properties: if you have two poly numbers P and Q and you take m times (P + Q), you get mP + mQ. If you have two numbers m and n and you multiply a poly number P first by n and then by m, it's the same as multiplying P by the product mn. And if we have m times (P times Q) where P and Q are both poly numbers, that's the same as (mP) times Q, which is also the same as P times (mQ) (I didn't have room to write it). So this is a useful additional operation that involves not just poly numbers but also numbers as well. We should be careful to distinguish between poly numbers and ordinary numbers; technically, they're different objects. This idea of scaling poly numbers gives us an alternative way of thinking about multiplying two poly numbers, which makes it very parallel to Hindu-Arabic multiplication. For example, if you're multiplying (2, 3) by (1, 0, 4), the result, which is over here (2, 3, 0, 12), can also be obtained by taking this one and multiplying it first by (2, 0, 0), giving us (2, 0, 8), and then multiplying it by (0, 3), which has the effect of multiplying each of the entries by 3 and also shifting everything down one. So we've got 2 times (1, 0, 4) plus 3 times the shifted-down (1, 0, 4). Why is that the same as Hindu-Arabic multiplication? Well, suppose we wanted to multiply the numbers 401, which is one read up, times this number 32. In ordinary multiplication, we would use this 2 and multiply by 401 to give us 802, and then we would multiply by 3 (which is 0, 12) and shift everything over one because we're actually multiplying by something in the tens spot. Then we add the ones, tens, hundreds, and thousands. Normally, there being 12 here is not allowed; we would ordinarily carry something over to the next spot, but in this setup, what we're going to do is just allow the entries of each position to be arbitrary natural numbers, not restricted from 0 to 9. So that's essentially what we're doing when we're doing this kind of multiplication. We're just doing Hindu-Arabic multiplication allowing arbitrary numbers in each of the slots. Here's another example: you can check that, say, (3, 2, 5) times (1, 4, 0) is obtained by first multiplying this by 3, multiplying it by 2 and shifting down, multiplying this by 5 and shifting down two, and then adding all the way across like that. Here's a very easy example with a special poly number (0, 1), just noting that its effect on another poly number is simply to shift it down one. This poly number is the simplest poly number which is not itself essentially a number, and it's going to play an important role as we carry on. Now let's talk about associativity of multiplication. Suppose we have three poly numbers P, Q, and R with entries p_i, q_i, and r_i. The product PQ is another poly number whose i-th entry is given by this formula: the sum over all k's and l's, positive numbers, of p_k times q_l where k + l sum to i. Alright, so now suppose we have these three poly numbers, we multiply them all together in the order PQ and then R. Well, the i-th entry of that is, according to this rule, the sum over all k and l where k + l equals i of the k-th entry of P times Q times the l-th entry of R. Now, what's inside here itself is going to be replaced by this thing right here: the sum over all, say, n and m summing to k of p_n times q_m. So this is a double summation, a little bit complicated. But when you stare at it, you really have these three numbers n, m, and l, and they have the property that they add up to i. We're really multiplying p_n times q_m times r_l over all such possibilities. When we've written it this way, we see that it's now symmetric; it'll be the same as what we get when we expand this thing out: P times (QR)'s i component. That's essentially a sketch of the proof of why associativity works. To give you a sense of what this formula means, let's have a look at a few examples. Here we're multiplying three poly numbers: (1, 2) times (2, 5) times (4, 3). That formula that we had at the end of the last slide tells us the way we do this as follows: to find the 0th entry of the result, we take the product of all the entries whose indices sum up to 0, and there's only one way of doing that: it's the 0 plus 0 plus 0 makes 0. So there's 1 times 2 times 4; we get 8. To get the first entry, we have to look at products of three things: one from here, one from here, one from here so that the sum of the indices is one. That could be 0, 0, 1, or it could be 0, 1, 0, or it could be 1, 0, 0. There are the three products that we have to add up correspondingly, giving us 42. The second-degree term is obtained by taking products of three things so that the sum of the indices is now 2. That can be obtained by taking 1 times 5 times 3 (that's degree 0, that's degree 1, that's degree 1, so that has a total degree of 2 when we multiply), or we could take 2 times 2 times 3, or we could take 4 times 2 times 5. We add those three possibilities up; we get 67. Finally, the degree 3 term: there's only one way of getting a degree 3 term, which is to take the degree 1 term times the degree 1 term times the degree 1 term. This product that we've just done corresponds to the usual kind of polynomial product: multiply (1 + 2x) times (2 + 5x) times (4 + 3x), and we're getting that polynomial there. Here's another example that you can check the details to make sure you understand how this procedure works. So now that we've got some familiarity with poly numbers, it's time for us to say now exactly what is a polynomial. We'll do that next time. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger, and thanks for listening. [Music]

# 060 - What exactly is a polynomial？ ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 60 ｜ N J Wildberger [Sbet5GhqmyI].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today in this series on the foundations of algebra, we're going to answer the very important question of what exactly is a polynomial. A polynomial is a fundamental building block of algebra, and in most treatments of the subject, it's introduced only in a vague and imprecise way. But fundamental building blocks of mathematics have to be defined very precisely. So I remind you that the usual idea is that a polynomial is some kind of expression involving an unknown variable or general variable, x, or perhaps y, or perhaps a, or perhaps b. And this polynomial is allowed to have various manifestations or different expressions. The idea is that it should be built up just from the three operations: addition, subtraction, and multiplication. So these are all considered polynomials in x in the usual formulation of things. Another approach is to think of a polynomial as a function—something that inputs an x and outputs another number given by an expression like this. Alright, so our approach is going to be a little bit different. Roughly, our idea is that a polynomial is a polynomial form; in fact, it's a particular form that involves linear combinations of something we're going to call alpha. I'll tell you what alpha is in a second, but here are some examples of the kind of thing that we're interested in establishing. So this would be something like a polynomial P = 3 + 5α + 27α². It’s what you would usually think of as a polynomial in alpha, but we'll prefer to write this rather in this more laborious fashion. So instead of 3, we'll write 3α⁰; instead of α, we'll write α¹, so that the powers of alpha are increasing from 0 onwards. That's what a polynomial for us is going to look like. And now I have to tell you how do we connect this to poly numbers and what is this object alpha. We've made the observation before that a poly number with just one entry at the top, the degree zero spot, acts essentially like a number. The arithmetic for those poly numbers is just the same as the arithmetic for ordinary numbers. So the first poly number that goes beyond that arithmetic is this one right here, the poly number (0, 1), and that's what we are calling alpha. So alpha for us is just a particular poly number—that one right there. Alright, then it's easy to check that α² is the poly number (0, 0, 1), and α³ will be (0, 0, 0, 1), and in general, any power of alpha, say α^k, will be a string of k zeros and then a 1. That means we can rewrite a general poly number as a linear combination of these powers of alpha. For example, (2, 7, 0, 3) can be written as 2α⁰ + 7α¹ + 3α³ (we've left out the α² term because its coefficient is zero). This poly number (0, 0, 0, 13, 0, 52) can be written in a rather short way by just ignoring the zeros and writing 13α³ + 52α⁵. And so we say this is the standard form for a poly number, or if we want to be more specific, the standard alpha form, where we write out the poly number in powers of alpha and we use this convention for α⁰, α¹, α², α³, and so on. We agree to leave out the coefficients with zero, as in this case here, where we don't write 0α⁰ + 0α¹ and so on, except in one special case—the zero polynomial. We'll write that as 0α⁰. We have to write something, and so that's what we'll write. So you can see one of the advantages of this standard alpha form for a poly number, namely that when the poly number has lots of zeros in it, we save ourselves the trouble of having to write all those zeros. We're just concentrating on the terms which have strictly positive entries, nonzero entries. So here then is our main definition: a positive polynomial is a positive poly number written in standard alpha form. So a polynomial is just a poly number written in a particular way. This is then not a new mathematical object; we're still just talking about positive poly numbers, but we're writing them in a new way. We've seen this before, actually. When we started off this series, we defined natural numbers as a string of ones—I hope you remember that was the definition of what a natural number was—but then at some point, we changed in practice to writing a natural number not as a string of ones but in terms of the Hindu-Arabic system. So now, when we write a natural number like 345, we're not writing a whole bunch of ones; we're just writing 3, 4, or 5. But really, it's the same mathematical object, just written in a new way. It's convenient in mathematics to have alternate ways of writing sometimes the same mathematical object. So please stop the video now and have a look at these eight examples and try to figure out which of these are positive polynomials. Alright, I'll give you a moment to think. [Pause] Okay, so the answer is: only one of them. The first one is a positive polynomial, and the rest are not. Let's see why not. This one here has a negative sign. We so far have not introduced negative signs—all the coefficients have to be positive natural numbers. Later on, of course, we're going to include that as a possibility too, but right now, we're just talking about positive polynomials. What about this one? Well, we're being a little bit fussy here. This 4 does not have an α⁰ in front of it. We insist that all the terms have coefficients times powers of alpha. The trouble with allowing something like this is that it's easy to get confused between this number 4 being an actual number or being a poly number. We want numbers and poly numbers to be different objects technically, so that's why we're not going to allow this. How about this? This seems to be alright, but it's not because it's not written out in the standard form. The standard form requires that you start with the degree 0 term, then the degree 1 term, then the degree 2 term, and so on. Well, you might say, "Well, you could expand this," and you could, but that's not something that we want to do. If you give me an example of a positive poly number, it better be in the required form, unlike a computer; otherwise, I'm not going to accept it. So in other words, I'm not willing to do the work involved in expanding this out and putting it in standard form. The person coming with the object has to do that work themselves. What's wrong with 0? Well, we agree that 0 is going to be written 0 times α⁰ because it's level 0, so it should have an α⁰ in front of it. How about this one: α⁷ + 2α⁴? It's not in the standard form. The powers of alpha have to be increasing. αⁿ and α^m—these are variable things. They're not powers of alpha in the sense that we're talking about—there have to be natural numbers up here. How about 2α⁴ + α⁵ - α⁴? This negative sign means that it's not allowed, even though we could simplify this to α⁴ + α⁵. If we did that, then it would be a positive polynomial, but right now, it's not. So we're being very fussy, we're being very clear. We're only allowing objects that fulfill the specifications of the definition. This is a very good attitude to have when you're laying the foundations for mathematics. Next time we're going to talk about factoring positive polynomials or positive poly numbers. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music]
