# 061 - Factoring polynomials and polynumbers ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 61 ｜ N J Wildberger [iX2xWcm57uQ].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today we're going to talk about factoring polynomials and poly numbers. First, I'll review some basic facts about factoring with ordinary natural numbers. So, a few definitions just to remind you: if 2 times 3 equals 6, we say that 6 is a multiple of 2 or a multiple of 3, and we say that 2 and 3 are divisors or factors of 6. If we take a bigger number like, say, 1001, then it could be factored in a number of different ways to be written as 77 times 13, or 7 times 143, or as 1 times 1001—that's also a factorization. So although it may appear that there are lots of different ways of factoring a number, there's a very important and fundamental theorem called, in fact, the Fundamental Theorem of Arithmetic, which says that every natural number can, in fact, be factored uniquely—in other words, in only one way, up to order—into prime factors. A prime number, I remind you, is a number that only has two factors, namely 1 and itself, for example, 3, 5, 7, 11, 13, 17, 19, and so on. So the Fundamental Theorem of Arithmetic means that we can express every natural number as a product of prime numbers, and there's only one way of doing that up to the order in which we present those primes. So, for example, 1001 is 7 times 11 times 13. 1002 is 2 times 3 times 167. 267 is prime. 1003 is 17 times 59. 1004 is 2 squared times 251. There's no reason why a prime factor can't appear more than once in the factorization of a number. There's 1005 and 1006, and this already indicates a major interesting aspect of this: that factoring large numbers is highly unpredictable. It's not at all obvious how to extend this table to the next twenty or thirty. There are a few patterns that are kind of obvious—I mean, obviously, every even number is divisible by 2, and if this number is divisible by 3, then the one after it is also going to be divisible by 3—but aside from those relatively trivial remarks, it's very hard to predict how a random big number factors into primes. And that's a major important fact when it comes to encoding or encrypting information, say, on the internet. This unique factorization theorem plays a very important role. Alright, so we're not going to prove these facts. These are kind of standard facts—there's nothing problematic about them, I think, once you've set up arithmetic as we have done. But we are going to be interested in extending this kind of thinking to poly numbers and polynomials, which are, as we've seen last time, just certain types of poly numbers. The poly number (1, 2) times the poly number (3, 5) is the poly number (3, 11, 10). So we say that (3, 11, 10) is a multiple of (1, 2) or a multiple of (3, 5). Now, we say that (1, 2) and (3, 5) are factors or divisors of (3, 11, 10). And a natural question is, suppose we were given (3, 11, 10)—how could we find its factors? Well, for that, it helps to look at the general formula. If we multiply the binomial (a, b) with the binomial (c, d), we get (ac, ad + bc, bd). That's a trinomial. So if we stare at this, it helps us solve this problem. For example, suppose you're given the trinomial (7, 45, 18), and you want to write it as (a, b) times (c, d) for some (a, b) and some (c, d). Well, then ac has got to be 7, and these are natural numbers. a and c are either (1, 7) or (7, 1). In other words, the set {a, c} has got to be equal to the set {1, 7}. Similarly, bd has got to equal 18. So now there are more possibilities because 18 is not prime; they could be (1, 18), (2, 9), or (3, 6) in either order. And there's one more piece of information: the middle term, ad + bc—this times this plus this times this—has got to equal 45. And now it's a standard kind of little game at one place, looking at all the possibilities. How could we choose these two numbers? Now, how do we choose a pair here so that the a times the d plus the b times the c should equal 45? Well, there's only going to be one way, and that turns out to be the solution. So we tried 1 times 9 plus 7 times 2—well, that wouldn't work. We've got 1 times 3 plus 6 times 7—1 times 3 plus 6 times 7—that would work, giving us 45. And so that's how we get the factorization, by essentially looking through all the possibilities encoded by these 3 equations. In more familiar high school terms, what we've done is we've taken the trinomial (7 + 45x + 18x²) and we've factored it into two binomial expressions: (1 + 6x) and (7 + 3x). We rather think of this expression as representing this equality between this polynomial and these two polynomials—that's really the same thing. So at this point, it's probably a good exercise for you to take a few binomial poly numbers and multiply them to get a trinomial, and then put it aside for a day or two and then come back and see if you can factor those trinomials—go reverse the process to find the factors. Some trinomials cannot be factored. If you just write down a random one, then it's very likely that it cannot be factored. For example, it's not hard to see that the poly number (1, 5, 1) cannot be factored into two binomials. So the question is, should we call that kind of poly number a prime polynomial? However, an interesting complication arises when we move from binomials and trinomials to slightly larger poly numbers, and here's a very interesting example: the poly number (1, -1, 1), which is itself a trinomial, times (1, 1, 1) is (1, 0, 1, 0, 1). So this poly number, which is a positive poly number, factors, but one of its factors—this one here—is a poly number with a negative coefficient. Up to now, we have not been including this possibility; we've only restricted ourselves to positive coefficients. But it turns out that if we do that, we miss out on this phenomenon here. And this is something that's quite different between polynomials or poly numbers and ordinary natural numbers. For ordinary natural number division or factorization, negative numbers play no essential role. However, for poly numbers, they play a very important role. Certain factorizations are possible with negative coefficients that are simply not possible if you restrict to positive coefficients. So the mathematics is forcing us to enlarge our view. We're going to have to move from positive poly numbers to talking about integral poly numbers. And what is an integral poly number? It's an expression just like a positive poly number, except that the entries are allowed to be integers. This defines a new mathematical object called an integral poly number, and we'll give it this title: poly-int, for integral poly number. This is a very nice realm for doing arithmetic with poly numbers. It's, in fact, better in many ways than sticking to positive poly numbers. So we're going to have to do that now. We're going to have to rethink what we've done, move to integral poly numbers, and investigate the arithmetic of these. When we do that, we will see that primes and fundamental theorems come into focus for us, and it becomes very rich and beautiful. So next time, it's arithmetic with integral poly numbers. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music]

three with a decimal point there and so not written so if we take this expression and divide both sides by a hundred we see that fourteen point two three is the same as one thousand four hundred and what is that as a fraction well we want to move that decimal point over three places to do that we'll multiply it by a thousand multiplying it by ten moves it over one by 10 and again moves into over again 2 by 10 again moves it once more so when we multiply by a thousand we get 14 and so now when we divide both sides of this by a thousand we see that 0.01 4 is 14 1000s now I hope what's becoming attended students given degrees etc but at the level of a logical discipline modern mathematics is very very weak and in fact there are such gaping holes in it that you could drive trucks through it # 072 - Graphing polynomials ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 72 ｜ N J Wildberger [PXElyy86m9c].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today we're going to look at graphing polynomials. This is a way of associating a visual geometrical object with what's up till now been a purely algebraic object. Once we do that, we can interpret the tangents that we defined last time—the various key tangents of a polynomial: first tangent, second tangent, third tangent, and so on. We can interpret those geometrically as tangent lines, tangent conics, which are approximating the polynomial at a point. Now, I'm going to increasingly be using the "polynomial" word rather than the "poly number" word to make contact with more classical mathematics, but I hope you keep in mind that for us, a polynomial is just another word for a poly number. So, when it comes down to it, a polynomial like this is essentially just a list of numbers—the list of the coefficients of the various powers of alpha. And today, we're going to be talking about just those polynomials with integer coefficients, so-called integral polynomials or integral poly numbers. So our idea of representing such a polynomial graphically is to plot the values of pairs of numbers and the polynomial values at those numbers. That way, we get a two-dimensional representation that somehow incorporates or allows us to visualize the polynomial. Let's start off by having a look at this particular polynomial and calculating some of these values of the polynomial at various constants. Here, on the left-hand side, I've listed various integers from -5 up to 10, and I've computed for you the values of this polynomial at the various constants. So, this -166 here means that if you replace alpha with -3, then this evaluates to -166. So, our idea is to graph pairs of such numbers in a two-dimensional setting, and for that, we're going to go back to Cartesian coordinates. This is a way of introducing geometry in a very concrete and explicit way, using essentially a sheet of graph paper with equally spaced horizontal and vertical lines forming this pattern of unit cells or squares. We can write a distinguished horizontal line and call it the x-axis (the usual convention), and we can distinguish a particular vertical line and call it the y-axis. We can label these various nodes here: 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, and so on in that direction, -1, -2, -3, and so on in this direction, and going up: 1, 2, 3, 4; going down: -1, -2, -3, and so on. The idea is that if we have a point like this one here, then we can look at the x-coordinate, which is 1, and we can look at the y-coordinate, which is 6, and then we can essentially give this point a name by associating it with the pair (1, 6) enclosed in square brackets by convention. So, we can define a mathematical object called an integer point, which is just a pair of integers enclosed in square brackets, and it's an ordered pair. So, there's a first one and a second one corresponding to the x-coordinate and the y-coordinate. That defines a new type of object, which we might call "Int²," an integer point. And then a variant on that is when we do exactly the same thing but we allow the numbers in the brackets to be rational numbers. In that case, we get a rational point, and this new type of object we can call "Rat²" or, for simplicity, just a point. So, a point is generally just a pair of rational numbers. So, there's (0, 6); there's a point (0, 3); there's (-1, 0); there's (-2, -3). We can also talk about points that are not necessarily on the intersections, but sort of with more random positions. One of the lovely things about this Cartesian coordinate system, essentially introduced around the time of Descartes, is that we can encode lines and other nice curves, like conics, pleasantly in an algebraic form. So, for example, this line here, which goes through these various points—equally spaced: 1 over 3 up, 1 over 3 up, 1 over 3 up—so this really is a line. This line can be described by the relation between the x- and y-coordinates of the points on it. A usual kind of description is in terms of an equation that we can write like this: in this case, 3x - y + 3 = 0. Every point on this line happens to satisfy this equation. So, for example, (1, 6)—if you replace x with 1 and y with 6, then you get 3 - 6 + 3, which is indeed zero. So, this point satisfies the equation, telling us this point is on the line. You could check that all these points lie on the line in the sense of satisfying the equation. Now, the x and the y are in some sense arbitrary; they're not really necessary. The central ingredients are the various coefficients that appear here, and we like to abbreviate them by just talking about their proportion: 3 to -1 to 3. That encodes the line just as well as that does, and the pointy brackets sort of alert us to the fact that we're talking about an Euclidean line. So, we can talk about lines, and we can talk about points in this Cartesian coordinate system. Now, maybe it's useful to say here that the algebraic side of things—when we're talking about points like this or points like this and lines like this—doesn't actually require a picture. The picture is a physical object that is, strictly speaking, outside the world of mathematics. It resides here in the physical world in which we live. We're making various assumptions when we work with a picture like this, and we're also disregarding certain subtleties. For example, we're not fussing about exactly how wide these lines are, we're not fussing too much about exactly how big the points are that we're drawing, and we're also kind of assuming that it is possible, in fact, to create a pattern like this where these lines actually are all equally spaced and, by implication, where they're all parallel. So, even if we were to extend these lines indefinitely, they would not meet—it's our idea of parallelism. The same for the vertical lines. But that's really an assumption about the world in which we live, which may or may not be completely true. It's a kind of an important point. So, let me just say a little bit more about that. Imagine for a second that we're living on a round planet. Well, okay, we are, but imagine that this round planet was completely smooth and didn't have any objects on it other than perhaps ourselves. Otherwise, it was a completely smooth, featureless Earth, for example, perhaps something like this. So, imagine this is a model of the Earth, and perhaps you and I are right there—we're very, very tiny—so that as we look at the Earth, it's locally quite flat and we see what looks to us like a plane. Now, suppose that we attempted on this surface to draw such a grid plane. That would be a natural thing to do. We might make some strings and try to make some parallel lines, okay? And then some ones which are perpendicular, parallel lines like this. But if we started examining the geometry of this grid plane that we're making on the surface of the sphere, we would discover that it differs from this one subtly in measurable ways. In other words, the lines that we are, for example, thinking are parallel—if we actually stand to them, we would find very likely that they meet. Or, we would find that these two lines are perpendicular to this one, but if we move this line up and say to that one there, then these two lines are no longer perpendicular to it, which is quite different from our assumptions about this thing in the Euclidean plane. So, in other words, there are assumptions about the physical world built into a model of this graph paper that we have in our booklets. It's not entirely obvious that a piece of graph paper exists. Well, yes, of course, it looks like it exists in your notebook, but if you made a very, very careful measurement of the various relationships between these lines and the various perpendicularities, you might find that the curvature of the universe somehow makes them not as perfectly aligned as we think they are. There's a bit of a can of worms that we have to recognize that we don't really know the true geometry of the world in which we live, and since the 20th century, Einstein has taught us that the geometry of the world in which we live is, in fact, much more complicated than we used to think it is. There is such a thing as curvature, and the true overall structure is far from settled, but in particular, it's probably pretty obvious that it's not completely Euclidean and straightforward the way people used to think 100 or 200 years ago. So we have to separate in our minds the mathematical model that we're working with, the theory that we're developing, and these pictures that we're drawing to help us visualize the mathematics. This is an important point—that for us, the expressions that we will write down will be primary, logically. They take precedence. These pictures are just for us aids in understanding things, and we must be careful not to too quickly draw mathematical conclusions from the pictures that we draw. It's something to be aware of—a potential difficulty with relying too much on pictures. So this is the point I've just made: that the physical existence of a sheet of graph paper is separate from the mathematical model of integer points or rational points that we can work with algebraically. And there are empirical questions or technological issues associated with the graph paper and constructions on it. For example, is a sheet of graph paper really Euclidean? That's the issue we've already talked about. Do we live in a curved world? How does that affect the actual properties of a piece of graph paper? Another question is, can we extend such a sheet of graph paper? In many of our constructions, we might be interested in extending the axes, perhaps quite a long way to deal with very big numbers or very small negative numbers. So, a natural question is, to what extent can we extend this graph paper? Obviously, there are physical barriers because we're living on a planet Earth of only a certain size, and of course, in practice, it's hard to get a piece of paper that's too big. But even theoretically, the large-scale structure of the universe may actually be kind of closed. There may not be room, even theoretically, in the universe to have a sheet of graph paper that, for example, extends indefinitely. Another sort of related or inverse question to that one is, can we subdivide a sheet of graph paper arbitrarily? Suppose we want to create a finer mesh by, say, subdividing each of our squares into ten little pieces, and then we want a yet finer one where we subdivide even those smaller squares into ten little pieces, and so on. Can we keep doing that? Well, theoretically we can, but in practice, empirically, in the world in which we live, once we get down to very small scales, the laws of physics start becoming quite strange, and we arrive at limitations of our ability to resolve quantities, and quantum effects start interfering with our measurement. So, in the real world, you can't keep subdividing things. Or, to put it another way, when you subdivide things, things change. The laws of physics start changing on you, and you enter a world that is quite different from the way it is in our experience. So there actually are quite deep questions of a physical nature—the physical nature of the continuum or the large-scale structure of the universe—connected with a sheet of graph paper and what we actually can do with it practically. So, one way of dealing with these issues is to say, all right, we're going to think of these graphs that we're going to draw and the pictures that we make as simply aids to our understanding, and that we're going to draw a line between the logic of what we're doing algebraically and the pictures that we're going to draw to help us understand things and maybe guide our intuition. Because we are actually very visual creatures, we have a lot of brain processing devoted to visuals, so it makes sense to use that; it's one of our strengths. So let's go back to our polynomial, this one here, and let's try to graph it. Here are these various values that we've already computed. So, here are now the pairs of numbers that we would like to graph—these are sort of integer points, and there's a range of them. And one thing that we see is that the y-coordinates, the second coordinates, are actually, well, they get quite big, especially big negative. So, if we're going to hope to picture them, we should scale our grid in a rather different way than what we did before. And we have to allow ourselves this flexibility to scale the two axes in different ways. So here, the x-axis has been scaled so this is one unit: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, and so on. However, the y-axis has been scaled dramatically different—this now represents 100. So: 100, 200, 300, 400, 500, that's 1,000, that's -500, that's -1,000. Now, I haven't drawn all the lines because to do so would require thousands of lines. So we're going to adopt the convention that we're going to forget about all the lines—horizontal and vertical—except for the two axes themselves, and we're just going to use parallel lines to locate the positions of points. So let's graph these points: -5, -192; so -5, and then -192 is about there; and then this point: -4, -484, about there; -3, -166; -2, -30; -1, 8; 0, 8 actually has the same y-value; 1, 6 going down just a little bit; 2, 24 up a little bit; 3, 320 up a little bit more; then down 4, -25; 5, -42; 6, -454; and 7, -116. So these various points that we've drawn now are, in some sense, a picture of this polynomial. Now, you might not be used to that kind of picture—you might be used to a more continuous picture—but if we're working over the integers, which we can suppose we're at least initially doing, then this is a very reasonable picture, because in our minds there are only integers, and we're just plotting various integer points. Of course, we're not plotting a lot of integer points—we're not plotting any of those points with an x-coordinate more than 6 or a y-coordinate less than 6. So in fact, this is just some very small fraction of a much bigger picture that we could extend far in either direction if we had the patience and the room to do so. And maybe also the computing power, because perhaps it would get quite involved to compute these things if the x's become very big or very big negative. So this is a graph; it's not the only graph that we can draw. It's a kind of a partial graph—it only encodes part of the picture—but nevertheless, it's some kind of physical representation that we can look at and say, ah, there's some kind of pattern here. It's almost just like some dynamical thing moving. We might say it's an integral graph because all the points that we're plotting have integer coordinates. But a lot of times we are interested in not just integral values but also rational values—in other words, points with rational coordinates. So let's say a few words about them. Suppose, for example, that we're interested in the standard grid plane here, where now the x-axis is here, the y-axis is here, and suppose we're interested in, say, plotting a point like this with rational coordinates: 9/2 and 5/3. 9/2, 5/3—well, one way to do that is to say 9/2 is exactly halfway between 4 and 5, so we'll draw some point halfway between 4 and 5, and 5/3 is, well, somewhere between 1 and 2, and we'll do it roughly. But we would like to do it exactly because we would like to be completely correct here. So how do we construct such a point? It's important to realize that we can construct such a point with just lines connecting existing points in our grid plane. So I'm going to show you how to do it for this point, and I hope you'll see the pattern. To get 9/2, first of all, on the x-axis here, what I'm going to do is I'm going to consider a rectangle—this one right here—which is 9 over and 2 up. So that point there is the point with coordinates 9/2, and I'm going to connect that point to the origin with this green line. Now, where that green line crosses the line x = 1, because this is exactly halfway, this will be exactly halfway there. So this will be the coordinate 4.5, or 9/2. In a similar way, if we want to get 5/3 in this direction, what I do is I make a rectangle that goes 5 this way and 3 this way. So this rectangle right here—this is the point with y-coordinate 5 and x-coordinate 3. I draw the line connecting the origin, and I see where that line meets the line x = 1. Now, in that point there—so what is its y-coordinate? Well, this is 1/3 of the way from here to here, that's 1/3 of the way, and so this will be 1/3 of the way to here. So that point there will be 5/3. And all we needed to do that was to connect points that are already in the grid plane to create that line and to create that line and see where those two lines cross—lines that are part of the grid plane. So once, of course, we have the x-coordinate here and the y-coordinate here, it's not too hard to make parallel lines which then give us the required point. So with this technique in hand, we can now extend our view of a polynomial. We can now say, all right, let's try to visualize a polynomial p by plotting many points of the form (c, p(c)), where c now are suitably chosen rational numbers in a certain chosen range. In other words, we don't have to stick with integer values of c—we can substitute more general rational values and plot those points. So now let's have another look at our favorite polynomial, this one right here. The existing points that we have already drawn, the ones with integer values in this range, are right there. Now I'm going to add some new points—the green ones—whose x-coordinates are 1/2, -1/2, 3/2, -3/2, in other words, sort of halfway between the various points that we already have. So if I plug in 1/2 into here and compute, I get 95/16. And when I plot that, there's the x-coordinate 1/2, 95/16—that's roughly about six, and so with those two coordinates, we can plot that point there. There it is there, right in that green. And I've also plotted -1/2, 159/16, 3/2, and 143/16, -3/2 and -49/16, 5/2, which is there, and -5/2, 303/16, -7/2—that's 3.5, that's this one over here with 191/16, and 9/2 and -961/16—that's just barely in our range. So we have these new green points now to augment the red ones that we already have. And now, because I'm interested in the rational graph, what I'm going to do is I'm going to do a time-honored technique that goes back to primary school, or probably even earlier: we are going to join the dots. We're going to take our ruler or our straight edge and just join these adjacent points, because they're all one after the other in a certain order, join them up with this black line—just straight line segments from one to the next—and then we get some kind of picture of a part of the polynomial. Of course, this is not a complete picture; it's not complete for two different opposing reasons. It's important to appreciate both of these ways in which this is not complete. It's not complete, first of all, because we only went from here to here. All the values to the right here, all the values to the left here were not included in the picture. We could have included some more of them by making the paper larger or perhaps by scaling our axes appropriately. But no matter how we do that, no matter how big a piece of paper we have, no matter how much we scale, we still only get always a finite part of a bigger picture. It's always possible to extend the picture, so we never get the whole picture this way. There's another way in which we don't get the whole picture: because we've only subdivided each one of these intervals into, in this case, two pieces. Now, I could come along and say, well, I'm going to do a better job; I'm going to subdivide each one of these intervals into, say, ten pieces. So I could calculate ten different values and plot them all; then I would get a much finer resolution. But still, it would only be a partial picture. There's still a resolution here, and between the points that I'm plotting, I'm not really getting the accurate values—I'm replacing the values with straight line segments. Actually, if you have a computer program that plots curves like this, it might even dispense entirely with drawing little line segments. If the number of points is sufficiently large, if I divided each one of these pieces into, say, 100 and plotted all the points, then those points would probably run together, forming what looks to you like a continuous curve. There'd be no need to make little adjoining lines. So, in fact, mathematics programs that do make calculations do almost exactly this: they use a "join the dots" method, or they just deal with dots themselves, sort of a pixel approach or like a vector graphics approach. But the important thing to realize is that even if the picture that your computer is outputting looks very smooth, it's an illusion. It is not the real picture; it is a picture that has been derived by going to a certain resolution and no further—just the way it is. It's just as impossible to plot all the points in one of these little intervals as it is impossible to plot all the points on the whole line, whatever those terms even mean. We can go a certain way in both directions, and that's just the natural limitations of this mathematical problem. So this picture here that I'm drawing is actually very representative of what actually happens in practice, and there's no need to be ashamed of drawing straight line segments. That way, we stick with something that's completely concrete. I could have tried to sort of artistically draw a curve through there, but that would be a bit sloppy because I would probably not do a very good job, and also, if I did it again the next day, the curve that I drew would be probably noticeably different from the curve I drew today. So it's not really reproducible, and it's also very difficult to specify to you what exactly I have done—sorry if I'm taking a picture—but here, you can specify this and pass the instructions over the internet to someone else, and they can create more or less the same diagram, subject to the actual widths of points and widths of lines that you're going to use. So this is the essence of plotting polynomials. Now, let's turn our hand to some interesting applications involving tangents. At the end of the last lecture, I gave you an exercise to do, which I strongly encouraged you to do, which was to calculate various tangents to this cubic polynomial: 5 - 3 alpha - 4 alpha^2 + alpha^3. So let's have a look at some of those tangents, and you can compare with what I've got to see if I've gotten the right answer. And I'm also going to graph not just the original cubic but also various tangents. The original cubic p is plotted here, so this sort of signifies the coding: the red points and the black lines. I'm just using integer points here, so you can think of this, if you like, as being essentially over the integers—integral points—and I'm joining the dots between these integral points. And beyond these ranges, the numbers go off my scale, which goes roughly from 20 to -20, or maybe to around -30. Okay, let's consider the various tangents to this at the point zero. Those are the simplest to read off because they just correspond to truncating this expression in various ways. The simplest truncation is when we ignore all of those things, and we just get the zeroth tangent at zero, which is just five. Well, that's also a poly number or polynomial, and we can plot it also. It's very simple to plot—it just turns out to be this line. Why? Because we're plotting all the points of the form c and this thing evaluated at c, but this thing evaluated at c is always five, so we're plotting (1, 5), (2, 5), (3, 5), (4, 5), and so on—all of the points just along this line where the y-coordinate is five. So there's the zeroth tangent to this cubic at the point zero. The first tangent is what we get when we disregard everything except the constant and the linear terms—that's 5 - 3 alpha. That's a linear polynomial, and what does that look like? Well, it looks like this line right here with the blue dots and the red line. It is a line; it's the line y = 5 - 3x, if you like to think in terms of x and y. So, for example, when x is zero, then we get the value five; when x is one or alpha is 1, we get the value two; when alpha is two, we get the value -1, and so on. The second tangent to the polynomial at zero is what we get when we just drop that cubic term, so it's 5 - 3 alpha - 4 alpha^2, and we can plot that also for different values: -1, 0, -2, and so on. We can plug it into here and get the corresponding y-coordinate, and I've graphed them for you. So there's this point here, this one, this one, this one, this one, and this one—those are the values that are graphed in the range that I've drawn. So, to summarize, the original cubic is pictured by these points; the zeroth tangent is plotted by these points or that sort of horizontal line; the first tangent, or the tangent line, is plotted by these points; and the second tangent line, or the tangent conic at zero, is represented or graphed by those points. So you can see that this is a purely integral graph. This is a picture that you could draw even if you did not know what fractions were—if you only knew arithmetic with integers, you could draw this picture, and you can see some shape, and some patterns, even though we're only working over the integers. Now let's have a look at the same curve, but from the point of view of a different point—not zero, but we'll choose another point. Let's say the point two and see what the various tangents to the same curve look like at the point two—in other words, sort of at this point here. So the tangents at two I asked you to compute last time, and it turns out that the zeroth tangent at two is the constant -9, the first tangent at two is 5 - 7 alpha, and the second tangent at two is 133 - 15 alpha + 2 alpha^2, and I've plotted those for you: -9—that's the horizontal line consisting of these green points; the tangent line 5 - 7 alpha is that line there consisting of these points, and the tangent conic is, well, it has quite a different shape. In this case, it kind of a parabolic thing opening upwards, now still touching there. Also goes through these points. So those are the zero, first, and second tangents to the cubic at the point two. So, this is obviously so much fun you're going to want to do this yourselves. You've already calculated, I hope, the tangents to p at 1 and -1, as well as the ones that we've done today. So please construct integer graphs for the tangents to p at 1 and -1. So make exact corresponding pictures for the tangents at 1 and also at -1. It's very satisfying, and it's calculus! If you want to have something that is a little bit more smooth, you can do a little bit more work, and you can fill in some points by adding some more rational points, maybe at halves. If you want to be more ambitious, you can divide each one up into four, plot them all, get some nice pictures—everything can be done by hand, you don't need any artistic ability. Just remember we're only using line segments to join the dots. So this is pretty cool, and there's more cool stuff. In fact, we're going to see this cubic again—there's something really remarkable about this cubic, well, in fact, about cubics in general. But to prepare the way for that, I think next time we'll talk about lines and parabolas. The first tangents—tangent lines—are lines, they're pretty easy to understand. Tangent conics are always these parabolas, and we're going to have to know a little bit about parabolas. We'll study them algebraically and draw some pictures, so I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.

# 073 - Lines and parabolas I ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 73 ｜ N J Wildberger [QZt4G-fZjZo].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today we're going to look at lines and parabolas from the point of view of calculus. In a standard treatment of the subject, where you learn some analytic geometry involving coordinates x and y, we learn about the idea of expressing a linear relationship by a line in the plane. So, we talk about lines in the form y equals, say, 3x minus 4, or an alternate way of thinking about that is to rearrange things to bring everything on one side, say 3x minus y minus 4 equals 0. And that's not the only kind of line; we could also have a line that's just y equals something, like y equals 5, or say x equals 14. These are all examples of lines in the plane in an analytic geometry kind of framework. Going up one step, we get parabolas, which are second-degree curves in the form y equals, say, 5x squared minus 4x plus 1, or perhaps x equals 3y squared plus 2. Now, lines and parabolas are very familiar objects in high school, and students, of course, know lines look like this, and parabolas are some kind of U-shaped curves that are usually sort of rather quickly drawn, sort of like this, and they can be rather wide or they can be rather narrow like that. The kinds of parabolas that are studied here are rather of a special form; they're ones that are lined up either up or down. So, a parabola in the form y equals something like a quadratic expression in x would be something like this, or something that opens downwards, while this kind of expression usually represents a parabola that opens either to the right or to the left. Now, of course, parabolas are a special case of a conic section, going back to the ancient Greeks, the idea being that we take a cone, like this cone here in blue, and we slice that cone with a plane. And depending on the inclination of the plane to the cone, we get different kinds of conic sections. We could get ellipses or hyperbolas or parabolas, and the parabola is a very special case when the plane that we're slicing is parallel to one of the lines of the cone. That way, we get a one-sided figure, which is unbounded. If you move it just a little bit like this, you would get an ellipse; if you move it just a little bit like this, you would get a hyperbola with two branches. So, a parabola is a very special kind of conic section, sort of poised between the ellipses and the hyperbolas, just sort of on a knife edge between the two categories. From a geometrical point of view, a parabola appears as a rather special object, but from the point of view of analytic geometry that we're going to be talking about today, it's actually a very natural and fundamental kind of second-degree curve. Now, the usual story that you learn in high school and in college is that these things should be regarded as functions. So, we should think of y equals 3x minus 4 as a function of x, or we should think of y equals 5x squared minus 4x plus 1 as a function of x—in this case, a linear function, and in this case, a quadratic function. This is unfortunate, and I remind you that you could have a look at Math Foundations 42 A and B, where I discuss the problems—the very significant problems—with defining what exactly we're talking about when we're talking about a function. So, it doesn't make sense to think that we already have this idea of a function and then a linear function or a quadratic function is something contained inside that concept that's already well defined. So, our approach is going to be to not think in terms of functions. We're not going to take this point of view. We're going to rather think of lines and parabolas in this analytic geometry context as being essentially poly numbers—poly numbers of degree one and poly numbers of degree two, and that's all. So, they're not functions; they are just poly numbers. So, recall that a poly number, or a polynomial—pretty well the same thing—is constant precisely when its degree is zero. It's linear precisely when its degree is one, and it's quadratic precisely when its degree is two. So, a degree-zero poly number is just a single number in the poly number list, and in standard alpha form, we would just write that as a. A linear polynomial is actually a degree one—that's sort of degree zero, degree one—and we would write that in standard alpha form as a plus b alpha. So, a quadratic polynomial has a form where the c here in the second coefficient place is nonzero. In standard alpha form, that would be a plus b alpha plus c alpha squared. Now, it may take some little getting used to the way we're writing this line essentially in this parabola. So, the form here is a plus b alpha, not b plus a alpha. And here the form is a plus b alpha plus c alpha squared. So, if it was x instead of alpha, we would say a plus bx plus cx squared. It's much more usual to call the coefficient of x squared a, so the usual form for a parabola is ax squared plus bx plus c. Okay, so we're reversing the coefficients because we have this orientation that the coefficient-zero term is really where we start, and as we progress down the list, we're getting higher and higher coefficients, and the thing is becoming more and more complicated. So, for us, the start of the poly number is really here at the constant term, and that's why our indexing is going in this direction. We'll see later on that that's actually quite a useful terminology, notation, and also later on we may adjust things a little bit. Instead of using a, b, and c, we might use a0, a1, a2, and so on instead. Now, throughout this discussion, you have some choice as to whether you want to work over the integers or, say, over the rational numbers. So, you can think of these numbers a, b, and c as being integers or rational numbers, and typically, generally, we'll try to work in the rational number framework, but sometimes integers also. Most of our examples, the numbers will be integers. Alright, so we're going to look at these three sort of categories here, and naturally, the first one is to look at the constant polynomial. They are just essentially numbers, so the arithmetic of constant poly numbers is just exactly the same as the usual arithmetic of ordinary numbers. And when we represent a constant poly number in a graph, say this one here, the constant poly number 2, well, what are we doing? We're plotting points where the first coordinate is some number c and the second coordinate is the poly number evaluated at c. Now, if I evaluate this polynomial, the points that I'm plotting are going to have y-coordinate two—zero, two—one, two—two, two—three, two—two—one, two—two—two. If we had rational points, say in three halves, it'd also be three halves—two. So, all the plotting points end up being on this horizontal line. So, a constant poly number gets represented pictorially as a horizontal line. It's a line, but it's a special kind of one—it's a horizontal. So, when we go up one level to degree-one polynomials, we have a bigger story. There's more to say; we're talking now about linear polynomial lines. So, the typical one is something like ab in standard alpha form, a plus b alpha. And to ensure that the degree really is one, this constant b had better be nonzero. So, for example, 1 2, which in standard alpha form is 1 plus 2 alpha, that's a linear polynomial. But not quite every line, but most lines are linear polynomials of this kind. This one here—let me remind you how we plot it. We let alpha be various values, and we calculate the value of p for those various values, and then we plot the value c together with the value p of c. So, when c is zero, then the value is 1, so we plot 0 1. When c is 1, the value is 3, so we plot a 3. When c is minus 1, the value is minus 1, so we plot minus 1, and we get a sequence of points like this, moving each time one over, two up, one over, two up, one over, two up as we move to the right, and of course to the left, down to at every step. So, we're getting that line there. Now, the two coefficients, the one and the two, have a natural and important geometrical interpretation. What's the meaning of this one? Well, that's the value that you get when alpha is zero—in other words, it's the coefficient of y right above the x equals zero point. That's usually called the y-intercept. So, here the y-intercept is 1 because the zeroth coefficient of this poly number is 1. And what is the significance of the 2, the first coefficient? Well, it's the slope of the line. And what does that mean? Well, if we go over one in the x direction, then we have to go up two—go over one, up two—that means that 2 is the slope. It's how much you have to go up or perhaps down with every step of one over to the right. If this slope was negative, it would mean that if we go over one, then we would go down two. So, another way of saying what that slope is is that it's p of 1 minus p of 0. If you plug in 1 here, and then you plug in 0, and you take the difference, then these two—this one here—will disappear, and you'll just get 2. That's an algebraic way of saying. So, we have the y-intercept of p is p of 0, and the slope of p is p 1 minus p of 0. Okay, and in general, if you have a plus b alpha, then the y-intercept is this number a, and the slope is this number b. So, it's sort of interesting that originally when we were studying poly numbers, we didn't really think of these coefficients as having geometrical meaning. Now we see that when we're graphing poly numbers in this fashion, these numbers do have a very natural and useful geometrical interpretation. That's pleasant for us because we are visually oriented and we like to work with lines. Lines are very important kinds of mathematical objects. Let me point out that there's two kinds of lines that we're not getting here. First of all, we're not getting horizontal lines. They are not linear polynomials; lines are special because they're the cases where the b is zero—back to that earlier example of constants. That's a special case; that's actually degree zero. And the other kind of line that we're not getting is a perfectly vertical line like this. That's not represented by such a poly number because for every value of x, there's only one value of y that we plot, namely the value of the polynomial. So, a linear polynomial is non-horizontal and non-vertical, but any non-horizontal or non-vertical line can be represented as a linear poly number. So, now that we are representing poly numbers geometrically, we can apply geometrical concepts to poly numbers. And for lines, a very natural geometrical concept is the idea of parallel lines. What does that look like algebraically? Well, here's a line p, which is 1 2, or 1 plus 2 alpha, and here's a line q, which is minus 4 2, or minus 4 plus 2 alpha. These lines look parallel, and they are. Now we can see that by looking at their slopes. Actually, it's the slopes that determine parallel lines, because this 2 means that if we go over one, then we have to go up 2. And this 2 here means that if we go over one, we have to go up 2. So, the slopes are the same; those lines really are parallel. This one has a y-intercept of 1; this one has a y-intercept of -4. Another interesting geometrical notion is that of the x-intercept—the coordinate on the x-axis where the line crosses the x-axis. Now, for a linear polynomial non-horizontal, each one of them has to cross the x-axis somewhere, and to find that x-intercept, well, we're looking at the x value here so that the poly number's value at that point is zero. That'll guarantee that the point that we plot actually is on the x-axis—it has a y-coordinate of zero. So, we're really solving p(x) equals zero here. If we set this equal to zero and solve for alpha, we would get minus one-half, which is the coordinate there. And if we set this one equal to zero and solve for alpha, we would get 2, which is this coordinate here. Here's another pair of lines, r and s, and they also look parallel. And r is minus two-thirds, minus one-third alpha, which is now a little bit new because we've got some rational coordinates there. But we can rewrite this as a ratio of one integral poly number divided by another integral poly number—in other words, a rational poly number. So, this is just another way of writing this rational poly number. Let me remind you that we said that the rational poly numbers were the second most important field in mathematics because we can do all the operations within that context. So, this kind of object—the same things apply. It has a y-intercept of minus two-thirds, which would be about there. It has a slope of minus one-third, so that means if you go over one, you go down one-third—over one, down one-third—and its x-intercept here is minus 2. If you set this equal to zero and solve for alpha, you would get minus 2. And here is s, this one here, which is 2 minus one-third alpha. It's parallel to this one because the slopes are the same—they're both minus one-third. This one has a y-intercept of 2, and if you write it this way, you can see that the x-intercept is going to be 6 out here somewhere. So, the idea of parallel lines is very simple: you just look at the slope, which is the first coefficient. Now let's think about perpendicular lines, which, of course, is the other standard idea in comparing lines. What does perpendicularity look like in terms of the algebra? There are, in fact, quite a number of different possible definitions of perpendicularity. We're only going to talk about the standard one here, but if you are interested in other types of perpendicularities, some coming from a relativistic geometry, you can have a look at micro-metric geometry videos, which are in the WildTrig series from 53 to 57. There, there's not just a blue perpendicularity, which is the usual, but also a red and a green one. They interact in a remarkable way. So, our standard notion of perpendicularity—you'll agree this is perpendicular to this. And if you look at it, this vector here is the vector 2 1 in round brackets to denote a vector. Here's the vector minus 1 2, and these are perpendicular vectors. More generally, the rule is that to get a vector perpendicular to ab, you interchange them and put a minus sign in front of one of them, say minus ba. Now, over here, this line has a slope of one-half, because if we go over one, we go up one-half. And this line has a slope of minus 2. The one-half here is this divided by this, and the minus 2 is this divided by this. So, in general, the slope here will be b over a, and the slope here will be a over minus b. And so the relationship between those two slopes is that one is the negative reciprocal of the other. In other words, the two slopes, b and d, of the two lines—if we write them as poly numbers—will multiply to minus 1. Okay, so just make sure you agree here. The slope here is one-half, the slope here is minus 2; you multiply them together, you get minus 1. And that's the same as here and here—b over a times a over minus b—you get minus 1. This is coming from geometry, right? If we were just working with poly numbers, it didn't occur to us to say, "Oh, it's perhaps interesting if we have linear polynomials multiply to minus 1." It wasn't clear that that was an interesting thing to do. But now we see that with this representation of poly numbers as lines in the Cartesian plane, this condition is interesting because it corresponds to something that's very important geometrically—namely, perpendicularity. There is one more important thing to say about lines before we leave them, and that's a formula for the meet of two lines. So, we've talked about this before when we were talking about geometry in the Cartesian setting. We already talked about lines as geometrical objects, but they were things of this form here, and we talked about formulas for the meet or the intersection of two lines. So, it's a similar story here, but now we're talking about everything in terms of poly numbers. So, let's first of all introduce a notion of incidence. When a point, say xy, lies on a poly number p, we'll say that xy lies on p, which is a plus b alpha, precisely when y equals a plus bx. In other words, this is exactly the condition that this be a point that we use to plot this poly number. If we replace alpha with x, then we get p(x) right here. So, this is actually p of that, and we'll say that this point lies on the poly number. That's an algebraic reflection of the operation of drawing a point and saying that point lies on the poly number or is part of the graph. Now, this particular relation is a linear relation. It can also be rewritten as bx minus y plus a equals zero. And if you go back to Math Foundations 24 and have a look at lines, you'll see that this kind of linear equation is represented by the line that we called b 2 minus one-half a, with some pointy brackets representing a Euclidean line. So, whether we're talking about this poly number a plus b alpha or we're thinking geometrically about this line, we're talking about two things which are pretty close to being the same. They're not really the same, but we can certainly, in our minds, compare them and think of them as being effectively the same. So, in this video, we stated an important theorem—the meet of lines theorem—and now I'm just going to restate that theorem in terms of poly numbers instead of lines. So, if you have two poly numbers, p1 is a1 plus b1 alpha, and p2 is a2 plus b2 alpha, and they're not parallel, so in other words, the two slopes b1, b2 are not equal, then there is exactly one point which lies on them both—namely, here it is. Here's the unique point which lies on both of these two linear polynomial lines. The x-coordinate is (a1 - a2) / (b2 - b1). The y-coordinate is (a1 b2 - a2 b1) / (b2 - b1). And you can see why we require the slopes to be different because if they weren't different, we'd be having zeros here in the denominator, which is not good. Alright, so I'm gonna leave this to you as an exercise. Please compare this with the formula in Math Foundations 24 for the meet of lines theorem stated in terms of lines like that. It's very instructive to compare these things. Alright, so those are really the main things that high school students learn about lines. Lines are pretty simple things for us—they're just poly numbers with two entries instead of one. It's one step up from a number. Now it's time to go one step more, to go to poly numbers with degree 2. Now, that looks like parabolas. Going from lines to parabolas is a big step up. So, let's now introduce quadratic polynomials—more parabolas—and we're going to derive some of the main facts about them, which follows and in some sense is kind of a review of things that we've already done in the last few videos. So, I hope this is more or less familiar to you if you have watched those videos before, but perhaps they'll be a good summary and review of those facts. First of all, what is a quadratic polynomial? It's just a poly number of degree 2 consisting of three coefficients, a plus b alpha plus c alpha squared, and we insist that that third one, the c, be nonzero. And I remind you, once again, this is a somewhat non-standard form. Okay, in terms of x, this is a plus bx plus cx squared. And we'll make the same definition that we did for lines: we'll say that the point xy lies on the poly number p precisely when if you plug in x for alpha, you get y. So, y is equal to a plus bx plus cx squared. So, these are just exactly the points that we actually plot when we're drawing a diagram or a graph of the polynomial. We talked about the Taylor by poly number and the sub-derivatives—it's an absolutely fundamental kind of thing for calculus. Let's do it here in this quadratic situation where we can see everything explicitly, and it's instructive. So, what is p of alpha plus beta? That's the Taylor by poly number of p—it's what you get when you replace the alpha with alpha plus beta, going from a poly number to a bi-poly number. So, when a plus b alpha plus beta for c alpha plus beta squared, we expand that out—a plus b alpha plus b beta plus c alpha squared plus c times 2 alpha beta plus c beta squared—and then we group those various terms into the ones that don't involve any betas, the ones that just involve a single beta, namely this one and this one, and the ones involving beta squared, which is only this one here. We see the original poly number recovered, so this, what we call the 0th sub-derivative, is just the original poly number p. This one here is very interesting and important—this coefficient of beta is what we call the first sub-derivative, or just the derivative of p. Right, so the derivative of p, by definition, is the coefficient of beta in the Taylor by poly number, and there it is there. It's b plus 2c alpha, so it's this thing here without an alpha, plus 2c times alpha. And we also can read off the second sub-derivative, which is the coefficient of beta squared. In this case, it's just a poly number c, the constant polynomial. So, these are the two sub-derivatives of this quadratic polynomial; the other sub-derivatives are all zero past this point. Now, the Taylor by poly number was symmetric in alpha and beta, so we can interchange the alphas and betas in the previous equation and write it now as a combination of powers of alpha. So, we're just replacing alphas and betas in the previous. Well, now what we'll do in the next couple of steps is very important, okay, so make sure you understand what we're doing here. The next step is just a rewrite where we're gonna replace beta, which is a poly number, with a number, and let's use the number r. Previously, for a constant, I was using number c, but that's not going to work too well because there's already a c in our equation, and d is not going to work either because in a little while we're going to go to cubic polynomials, ds as well, and their coefficients. So, r is nice and safe—we're not likely to get up to that very soon. So, r is a constant; replace beta with r—just there's r, there's an r, and there's nothing there. Okay, so this is just another equation—this is now an equation just involving the poly number, a simple poly number in alpha. And now what we're gonna do is we're going to replace here alpha with alpha minus r—it's a translation of this. And we'll replace alpha with alpha minus r. Well, this becomes just alpha, and the alpha here becomes alpha minus r, and the alpha here becomes alpha minus r, and otherwise it's completely unchanged. So, just this changes, this changes, and this changes. But this is now a very important expression—this is the Taylor expansion of p at r. So, we're thinking about r as being a particular value on the line, and this is now a formula that tells us how to evaluate the polynomial at a random point alpha just in terms of the differences between alpha and r and the various powers of those differences. This is a very important formula, this Taylor expansion, and what it allows us to do, amongst other things, is to read off the various tangents to a poly number. So, for a quadratic polynomial, there's really only one interesting tangent, which is the first tangent, which is what you get when you look at all the things here of degree 1. So, the first tangent of p at r is this plus this. We're just dropping the quadratic terms. Now we do some arithmetic; we're going to expand this out as a poly number in alpha. It means we have to multiply this minus r here, so there's going to be a minus r times b here, which is conveniently going to cancel with this br here. So, there's still an a, but what happens to the cr squared? Why does it become minus cr squared? Well, it's because here also there will be a minus 2 cr squared. There's another term multiplying r there, so those minus two of them will subtract from the one of them to give minus 1 cr squared. And then the terms involving alpha will be just the way they were before—b plus 2c r times alpha. What is this? This is a linear polynomial—coefficients a, b, and c of the poly number, and the particular value at which we're evaluating the number r. So, this is a formula for the general first tangent, or the tangent line, to p at r. It's a useful and important formula because it tells us how to calculate quickly the tangent line to a parabola at any point. So, let's look at an example. Here is a poly number, a quadratic polynomial, 5 minus 4 alpha squared. Okay, so I'm going to leave it to you to check that the first derivative is minus 3 minus 8 alpha, and the second sub-derivative is minus 4—this one is half of the second derivative, it's minus 4. Okay, and here's that formula for the tangent line that we wrote down—that's the general formula for a general quadratic polynomial, get 5 plus 4r squared minus 3 plus 8r all times alpha. So, this is a linear polynomial—evaluating that. So, we can choose different values of r here, and for every one of those values, we can calculate a tangent line to this parabola. Here's the actual value of the poly number—a graph of the polynomial—a graph. As I explained last time, we are only going so far, and this is a rather crude one. I'm only looking at more or less half-integral values, and actually, the topic appears at minus 3/8—that's when the derivative is zero. Okay, so what do various tangent lines look like? Well, at zero—so, at this point here—we replace r with zero, we get 5 minus 3 alpha. And if we graph that, that's got a y-intercept of 5 and a slope of minus 3, so it goes down at minus 3, minus 3—there it is right there—that's the tangent line to p at zero. The tangent line to p at 1—let me check—there it is right there, it's 9 minus 11 alpha—that's the equation of that line. At minus 1, we get 9 plus 5 alpha. At minus 3/8, which is right here, we get 89 over 16. In that case, the first tangent becomes actually not a linear poly number but rather a constant poly number, so it's a special case. And in fact, this is a rather important point on this parabola, because that horizontal tangent line means that the parabola has a maximum there. So, the fact that we're not getting a linear poly number is corresponding to the fact that there's something rather special about the point minus 3/8 for this parabola. And at minus 3, right over here, the tangent line is 41 plus 21 alpha. Now, I want to point something out to you—a rather interesting little observation. Have a look at these two tangent lines—the tangent line at 1 and at minus 1. These two tangent lines have the property that their y-intercepts are both 9, which means that they meet right at this point here, right on the x-axis, as it turns out, right on the y-axis, at the point 9. Now, that might be a little bit interesting because the parabola itself is not symmetrical around this line—the maximum is actually right there. So, the thing turns out actually to be symmetrical there, as we'll see in the next lecture. But there's something curious about the fact that we go equal coordinates away from zero and take the tangents, we get their meeting at zero. You can check now that the y-intercepts of the tangent at r and the tangent at minus r are always equal, no matter what r is like. That's obvious from here because the y-intercept is this one here, and if you replace r with minus r, that y-intercept is not changing. So, it means that if we took the tangent at 3, just somewhere there, and this tangent at minus 3, that they are going to meet exactly on the y-axis. This was a little exercise—you can see if you can generalize that. What's really happening with this phenomenon? Can you state a more general fact? We'll see how you do next time. Next time, we're going to look at this parabola and problems in general in more detail. There's quite a lot of remarkable additional features that parabolas have—they're very fabulous mathematical objects, in fact. So, I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.
