# 074 - Lines and parabolas II ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 74 ｜ N J Wildberger [LQ6sCDd5Qmo].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. This is a series on the foundations of mathematics, and currently, we're investigating elementary calculus from the point of view of polynomials or poly numbers. This is a novel and much-simplified approach to the subject. In our last video, we had a look at linear and quadratic polynomials or poly numbers and their associated graphs. Today, we're going to carry on talking especially about parabolas or quadratic polynomials in more detail. So, let's summarize what we accomplished in the last few slides of the last video. We're talking about a quadratic polynomial; there it is—P equals just a list of numbers: a, b, and c, or in standard alpha form, a plus b alpha plus c alpha squared. Now, we're assuming this is really a quadratic polynomial, so that this term here, this c constant, is actually nonzero, so it really is degree two. We discovered by expanding the Taylor by poly number that the first derivative or the first sub-derivative was b plus 2c alpha, and the second sub-derivative was c. We also determined that the tangent line or the first tangent to the poly number at the point r is given by this expression. So, that's a line—a degree one polynomial—unless this is zero, which is an approximation to the poly number P at the point r. r is just some random number, and we're generally working over the rational numbers here, but a lot of what we say works for the integers as well. At this point, we can observe something reasonably interesting: the slope of the tangent line to P at r is exactly the derivative of P evaluated at r. So, we saw last time that when we had a line like this, the two constants here had geometrical significance. This was the y-intercept, and this was the slope. This quantity here, b plus 2c r, is exactly what you get when you take the derivative and evaluate it at r. So, this gives us a geometrical interpretation of what the derivative or the first sub-derivative means: it is the slope of the tangent line to the poly number or polynomial at the point r. So, for different points r, there are different tangent lines, and those tangent lines will have different slopes, and the slope at every point r is given by the derivative. A useful deduction to make from that is this theorem here: that the tangent line is horizontal precisely when r has the value minus b over 2c. Now, that's an immediate consequence of setting the derivative, which is b plus 2c r, equal to zero and solving for what r must be. This is a linear equation, so when we set it equal to zero, we are guaranteed to have a solution: it's minus b over 2c. This number is, as we'll see, very important in terms of the geometry of the parabola for the quadratic polynomial. And we can see that another way by doing something that we've already talked about in a former video in this series. If you look at Math Foundations 48B, we talked about completing the square in a quadratic expression. We're going to do that now with this quadratic polynomial a + b alpha + c alpha squared. I must warn you that our notation here is different from what it was there. This is a sort of non-standard notation; we're indexing our coefficients with a, the constant term, and c, the coefficient of alpha squared. Alright, so what we're going to do is we're going to rewrite this thing here, first of all by taking out a common factor c, which we're assuming is nonzero, writing it as c times (alpha squared + b/c times alpha + a/c). Then, in this expression here, we're going to add this term and also subtract it so that nothing actually has changed. Where did this term come from? Well, it's exactly half of this coefficient here, so we're taking half of this coefficient here and squaring it, and then also subtracting it as well. The reason why we're doing that is because now these first three terms here are actually a perfect square. This thing here is c times (alpha + b/2c) squared. The reason is simply that when we square this, we're gonna get alpha squared, we're gonna get b/2c squared, and twice the product will give us b/c times alpha—the thing that's right there. Of course, there's also the other terms: when we take them outside the bracket, multiply c by a/c, we get a, and when we multiply it by minus b squared over 4c squared, we get minus b squared over 4c. I'll write that just a little bit more compactly by taking this constant business over a common denominator of 4c. So, we get (4ac - b squared) all over 4c as the constant term there and c times (alpha + b/2c) all squared there. Now we make a simple but important observation that whenever we have a square, that square is necessarily positive. When I say positive, I generally mean greater than or equal to zero. So, this term here, if we substitute alpha for r, any number, then r + b/2c all squared will always be positive. So, I've repeated that formula that we've just obtained; we've rewritten the polynomial P in this fashion here. It's the same polynomial, it's just been rewritten. And now we're going to deduce some important things, so we'll put these together in a theorem: three different statements. The first is that if c is positive, then P(r) is bigger than or equal to this number for any r, with equality happening here precisely when r happens to be minus b over 2c. So, this is an inequality. If there was an equal sign, it would be an equality, and when we have an inequality like this, it's telling us this is always bigger than or equal to this. It's usually interesting to ask, well, what about the special case? When exactly are they actually equal? Because that's included here as a possibility. So, this second part of the statement is telling us that the equality occurs precisely when r has this value. Let's just have a look at that statement first: that's an immediate consequence of this. Because if c is positive, then this is positive, and we've just observed that this quantity here is always positive, so this is a positive thing. And so, if we add it to this, we're always going to get something which is bigger than or equal to this. That's the statement there. The value of the poly number, or the polynomial at r, is always at least this number here. And when do we actually get equality? Well, we get equality when this term is actually equal to zero, and this will be equal to zero precisely when the value r here satisfies r + b/2c equals zero—in other words, when r equals minus b over 2c. That's illustrated by this picture here. Okay, so here's a picture of such a quadratic polynomial where c is positive, so it opens up like this. We say it's a parabola opening upwards, and here's the value minus b over 2c. The function's value at that will be exactly this, because this term will be zero. So, there's the value (4ac - b squared)/4c right there. And geometrically, you can also see that the parabola is opening up—it's always bigger than or equal to this value, and it attains this value exactly when x equals this number here. That's what we're saying. Now, this number here is exactly the same quantity that appeared in our previous theorem, which stated that this tangent line here—the tangent to the polynomial at this point—is horizontal, which is a standard kind of important fact in calculus: that when you have a minimum here, the minimum is characterized by the tangent being horizontal. That's what we're seeing here. So, the second part of the theorem is what happens when c is strictly negative. If c is less than zero, then the claim is that P(r) is less than or equal to this quantity for any r, and again, equality happens exactly in the same situation when r equals minus b over 2c. And that's again a consequence of this formula because if this term here is now negative—less than zero—and this is always positive, then the product will still be negative, and so we're always going to get here as a total something which is less than or equal to this because it's this plus something negative. So, it's always going down, and it doesn't go down—we have equality precisely when this is zero. And that's the same condition as previously, when r is minus b over 2c. So, the picture that goes with that is this one here. It's a parabola opening downwards; there's the critical point minus b over 2c. At that point right there, the value of the polynomial is (4ac - b squared)/4c, and for all other values, it's something less—the value is less. And again, that's the place where the tangent is horizontal, the slope is zero. And there's a third part of the theorem: that the line x equals minus b over 2c is an axis of symmetry of the parabola. So, in this picture here, what we're talking about is this line here, the line where x happens to be this critical value, or over here, this line here. And the claim is that it's an axis of symmetry, and that means that if you take any point on the parabola here and you reflect it an equal amount on this side over here, you still get a point on the parabola, and conversely. So, it's like a mirror—if you put a mirror here, then the parabola would be reflected on itself. So, it's completely symmetrical in that line—it's called an axis of symmetry. And why is that? Well, it's again because of this expression here: that if you take any point where you, say, go r to the coordinates, so if you have some value x here so that you're adding r to minus b over 2c, then this quantity here will just be r. On the other hand, if you go r in the opposite direction, then this quantity will be minus r, and the reason why the two values are the same is because r squared and minus r squared are equal. That's the reason why the curve is symmetrical about this line. So, this is a very nice and important formula in the study of the geometry of a parabola or that quadratic polynomial. There's another very interesting and important consequence to this basic formula that we've established. There's the polynomial P, and there it's rewritten after having completed the square. So, the consequence of this is that we can actually say something about what the shape of such a graph is. The point is that the graph of P—or a graph for P, perhaps we should say because there's no unique graph—can be obtained from the graph of Q equals alpha squared, which is the simplest quadratic polynomial, by doing three things: by first scaling in the y-direction by some constant c—actually the same c here that's here—then translating by b/2c to the left, and then by translating up by (4ac - b squared)/4c. So, why is that? Well, suppose we take the polynomial alpha squared, if we perhaps that's right there and picture it right there—that's the standard parabola, usually y equals x squared in x-y terms. And it goes through the point (1, 1), it goes through the point (2, 4), of course, it goes through the point (0, 0)—it's symmetric along the y-axis. That's your basic parabola. So, if we take that thing and we scale it by multiplying it by c, what happens? Well, of course, it depends on what c is. But let's say c happens to be, say, 2. Okay, so that means that instead of plotting (1, 1), we would plot (1, 2). Instead of plotting (2, 4), we would plot (2, 8), and so on. So, all the y-coordinates would be times 2. The parabola would then look more constrained like this, but it would be basically a rescaling of this one. Okay, so that's one step—scaling, multiplying by c has this effect. On the other hand, if c happened to be, say, between 0 and 1, then the effect would be sort of the opposite—it would be more like going from here to here. The parabola would become wider, and if c was negative, well, it would have then again yet a different aspect, because then you'd be multiplying by minus 1, so the parabola, instead of opening upwards like this, would be opening downwards like this—that's the effect of multiplying by minus 1. So, after that rescaling, then what happens if we replace alpha with alpha + b/2c? Well, the effect is that instead of having things centered here at zero, it's now moved over to be centered at minus b/2c. So, for example, if b/2c happened to be positive, then we would be moving in this direction here. And why is that? The probably simplest way of seeing that is to ask yourself what happens if we evaluate this as at minus b/2c? If we evaluate this at minus b/2c, then we get zero, then we get just the same thing as we get if we evaluated this at zero. So, this thing evaluated there is the same as this thing evaluated there, so it means that the whole thing is shifted over so that its vertex is now at this point. Of course, this point doesn't have to be here—it could be on the right-hand side, it could be anywhere along the axis. Okay, it's starting to look a lot like our formula now. We just have to do one more thing: we have to add this, but this is some number that's independent of r, and the effect of it is simply to take the values and add this number to them. So, if this number was 5, for example, then the graph would just be obtained from taking this and adding 5 to the y-coordinates of all the points. So, it effectively just moves it up 5, or if this was minus 3, the effect would be to subtract 3 from all the y-coordinates, so the effect would be to move it down 3. So, you're actually moving it up by a certain amount. Alright, so that's a proof of this claim, and what it tells us is that all parabolas more or less have the same shape. Well, in a sense, the only real difference between the shape of the graphs is going from here to here in this rescaling. So, we're rescaling it, making it wider or narrower, and perhaps flipping it over. But then after that, we're not really doing anything to the shape—we're moving it around, translating it, but not changing its shape. So, all quadratic polynomials have graphs that are readily identifiable. You know what a parabola looks like. The story with cubics and quartics and higher poly numbers is much different, and that's going to be a very interesting aspect—it's an interesting question in algebraic geometry of what those things look like. But like lines, which are pretty predictable, parabolas are also very predictable, which is good. So, to clarify those translates and scalings on the last slide, let's have a look at an example. So, here is the poly number that we've looked at before: 5 - 3 alpha - 4 alpha squared. And here I'm going through the process of completing the square just so you can see how it looks again in this specific case. First, I take the minus 4 out as a common factor, so I get alpha squared + 3/4 alpha - 5/4, because I've taken a minus 4 out. And then in here, I look at this term here and divide it by 2 to get 3/8, and I square it and add it, and to keep things the same, I also have to subtract it as well. Then, these three terms here together form (alpha + 3/8) squared, and these two terms here—well, it would be x - 4, this thing here becomes just 5, and this one here is 9/64. If we multiply that by 4, we get 9/16 with a plus sign because there's a minus there and a minus there. So, when we simplify, we get -4(alpha + 3/8) all squared + 89/16. Okay, so how could we graph that from the point of view of starting with your basic parabola alpha squared? Now, here I've drawn a more standard kind of diagram where I've actually plotted points. So, this is the point (1, 1), point (2, 4), point (3, 9), minus 1, 1, minus 2, 4, minus 3, 9. There—our standard parabola—a graph of it. Admittedly, not a very refined graph, but a graph nevertheless. Alright, let's do these various steps. The first step is to multiply by -4. So, what effect does that have? Well, each of these points is multiplied by minus 4, and since that the y-coordinate is multiplied by minus 4, instead of plotting (1, 1), we're going to plot (1, -4), and instead of plotting (2, 4), we're going to plot (2, -16). And instead of plotting (3, 9), we'd plot (3, -36), but that's off our page, so we can't see it. So, we just get these few points, and we connect them up, and then that's our graph of -4 alpha squared. The next thing we have to do is to take care of this shift in here: we're replacing alpha with alpha + 3/8. What does that do to this thing? Well, it moves it over to the left—it's a translate to the left. Okay, there's a plus sign, so you might get confused and think about it moving to the right—no, we're moving it to the left. The graph here is to the left, and it's now got its vertex at -3/8. In your mind, you can check that because the value at -3/8, namely here, is going to be zero, so it really does go through that point there. And otherwise, we just take all of these points and move them over by -3/8 to get that one there. And then the final stage is to add 89/16, which is just a little bit more than 5. So, we have to add a little more than 5 to each one of these points, and we obtain this graph—that's our graph of the original polynomial P illustrating how it comes from alpha squared by a sequence of transformations: scaling, then a horizontal translation, then a vertical translation. Now we're going to have a few more slightly advanced theorems, which are often not found in high school courses or even in first-year or second-year courses at uni or college. It's a bit of a shame because these are natural, easy-to-prove consequences of what we've already done, and I think they're geometrically interesting facts about parabolas. Alright, the first one is that if we look at tangents to a general parabola or general quadratic polynomial, and those tangents are at r1 and r2, then the two tangents will meet at a point, and here is an explicit formula for where they meet. So, we'll have a look at the formula, and then we'll talk about some very interesting aspects of it. Okay, so what we're claiming is that if we look at the two tangent lines—the two tangent lines to P at r1 and r2—so here they were. These are the formulas that we had before, with r1 for the first one and with r2 for the second one—those are the two equations of the lines. We have to find where they meet, so we have to use the meet of lines theorem, which is a formula that tells us if we have equations for the two lines or linear polynomial x, where that meet is—this minus this over this minus this. Alright, what is this minus this? Well, the a's cancel, and we get cr2 squared minus cr1 squared. And what is this minus this? Well, now the b's cancel, and we get 2cr2 minus 2cr1. And the second term, the second coefficient, is this thing times this one—there it is there—minus this times this all over that same denominator that we had before, this minus this. Okay, so then what you do is you expand these two terms out, and it turns out that the numerators in both cases have a factor of c(r2 - r1), which you can cancel. And this thing here becomes (r1 + r2)/2. And then you can check—some exercise—that when you do the expansion and divide by the common factor, you can get a + b(r1 + r2)/2 + cr1r2. Now, the interesting point that answers an exercise that I gave you in the last video: the last video, I asked you to explain this phenomenon that two tangents were meeting above their midpoint. And here what we see is this a very general phenomenon: that the x-coordinate of the meet of these two tangents is always exactly halfway between the two coordinates. No, it's the average. And I should remark about this thing here—it's also rather pleasant. It has the same form as the original polynomial: a + b something + c something else. And this is a linear thing, and this is a quadratic thing, just like over here. But here we've got the average of the two r's, and here we have the product of the two r's. So, in some sense, it's almost the simplest kind of thing that you could cook up with r1 and r2 sort of involved in here. We're going to need one more result about lines that we didn't talk about last time. So, this is the theorem that tells us how to find the equation of a line between two points in terms of polynomials. So, if x1 and x2 are distinct, then the line P equals a + b alpha—in other words, the linear polynomial on which both (x1, y1) and (x2, y2) lie—is given by these two equations. So, this is the expression for a, and this is the expression for b. And they have very similar forms to the formula we were just using. The formula for a is (x1y2 - x2y1)/(x1 - x2), and b is (y1 - y2)/(x1 - x2). So, I remind you that these two numbers represent the y-intercept of the line as well as the slope of the line. So, this is a relatively familiar formula for the slope of the line between two points; this is perhaps a somewhat less familiar formula for the y-intercept of the line between two points. So, what's the proof? Well, we have to solve two equations. We are trying to find a + b so that this point and this point both lie on this linear polynomial—in other words, y1 equals a + bx1 and y2 equals a + bx2. We have to solve these two linear equations for a and b. That's about the level of the exercise. I'll leave you to do that to find that those are the solutions. So, we're going to apply that formula that we've just proven to have a look at the standard parabola, parabola P equals alpha squared, or in x and y coordinates, y equals x squared. So, there it is—I've drawn it a little bit freehand this time. It goes to the point (1, 1), through the point (2, 4)—it's a very beautiful curve. And we've chosen two points on it, a1 and a2, and the x-coordinate here is r1, and the x-coordinate is r2. So, this point is the point (r1, r1 squared), and this is the point (r2, r2 squared). And I'm interested in two things: I'm interested in the line that joins those two points, and I'm also interested in the two tangents to the parabola at those two points and where they meet. So, we know where they meet because we've just proven a theorem about that. And I remind you that they meet at—first of all, the x-coordinate is the average of r1 and r2, and the y-coordinate here is obtained from the poly number by taking a + b times this plus c times the product of r1 and r2. Well, this is a very simple form—it only has the c term, and the c is 1, so we're only getting r1r2 instead of those three things added up. So, this is the formula for the point where the two tangents to the curve at a1 and a2 meet. And I remarked once again that the x-coordinate here is exactly halfway between r1 and r2, because it's the average. Alright, what about the equation of this line? Well, we've just proven a formula for that. So, here are the two points. Then, let's say we say that the line is s + t alpha. Then the formula that we just wrote down was that s is equal to—how do you get that?—r1 times r2 squared minus r2 times r1 squared all over r1 - r2. So, here there's a common factor of r1r2, and the other factor is r2 - r1, which cancels with r1 - r2, leaving a minus sign. So, s is -r1r2. And what about t, the slope? It's r1 squared minus r2 squared divided by r1 - r2. Well, that factors immediately as r1 + r2. And so there is the equation for our line: -r1r2 + (r1 + r2) all times alpha. Now, it's a very pleasant relationship—the relationship between this line and this point. And in more advanced mathematics—and we'll be talking a little bit more about this—this is a very, very important relationship that somehow characterizes this conic. Okay, so if we move these two points around, and of course this line will move around, and this point here will also move around. And we say that the relationship between this line L and this point L is an example of polarity. So, we sometimes say that this is the pole of this line, and we sometimes say that this line is the polar of this point. We'll talk a little bit more about that in future videos—that's an important concept, so you're seeing it here for the first time in this simple case of this parabola. There are a few more things to point out here. One is that given this parabola, we can, in some sense, find addition and multiplication geometrically inside here. So, how is that? Well, suppose that you have two points, r1 and r2, on the line, and you wanted to add them. Well, one way of getting that sum, r1 + r2, would be to take r1 and r2, take the points on the parabola, draw this line, and look at its slope. Right, we know that r1 + r2 is the slope of this line. So, the addition—it's maybe a curious way of thinking about the addition—can be sort of done geometrically if you can read off the slope. What about the product? Well, you can see a product here. Okay, if you wanted to find what r1 times r2 was, one way of doing that would be to say, alright, we could take r1, r2, we can look at the two tangents, and we can see where they meet. And then the y-coordinate of that is going to be r1 times r2. Another way of finding the product is to look at this equation here. There's a product over floating around here. Now, what is the significance of this quantity? It's the y-intercept of this line. So, here's a kind of a curious sort of slide rule—a parabolic slide rule for multiplication. You want to multiply r1 times r2? You just go up to the parabola, you draw the line, you see where that line meets the y-axis—that's, well, not exactly the product; it's minus the product. So here, in this case, the product of this one times this one is pretty close to being minus two. So, both addition and multiplication are somehow encoded in the geometry of the parabola. While we're thinking about these two things, I'll give you some awesome exercises so you can have some practice thinking about this lovely geometry. So, the first exercise is very much in line with what I've just been talking about. It's another way of thinking about addition using the geometry of this standard parabola. So, I want you to show that if you're talking about the standard parabola, P equals alpha squared—there it is—and if you have two points on it, just as before, a1 at a2, now up here with coordinate r1 here and coordinate r2 here, and let's just denote the vertex by O for origin—that's got coordinates (0, 0)—then if you do the following: if you take the line through the origin, which is parallel to a1a2, okay, that's the meaning of this—it's parallel to here; it means it has the same slope—okay, then you find out where it meets the parabola in the second point. Then that x-coordinate there will be r1 + r2. It's another way of geometrically adding two numbers using the geometry of the parabola. This second exercise is a very lovely and important fact: that if A lies on the polar of B, then B lies on the polar of A. Okay, so here's a general parabola—it doesn't need to be the standard parabola, this works for a general parabola. So, you have a parabola here, and you have a point A, and that point A has a polar line—this one here. And I remind you, what that means is that if you draw the tangents to the parabola at the places where the line meets it, you get two. So, that's the polar of A. And there's another point B, and here in green is its polar. So, if you take the places where the green line meets the parabola and draw the tangents, you get two. Okay, the statement is that if A lies on the polar of B—which in this case it does—then B lies on the polar of A. This is a general fact—you can prove it just for the standard parabola. Those of you with a bit more algebraic skills, try to see if you can do it for a general parabola. Excellent exercise. And the third exercise relates again a chord of the parabola and the meet of the two tangents down here. So, if this point here has x-coordinate r1, and this point has x-coordinate r2, then we know that where these two tangents meet, that's going to have x-coordinate (r1 + r2)/2—the average of r1 and r2, or the place halfway between them. And now the exercise is to have a look at the corresponding point on the parabola with the same x-coordinate. So, you just go vertically up or down until you hit the parabola—you get a point. And the claim is that if you look at the tangent line at that point, it's going to be parallel to the line joining a1 and a2. This is not necessarily horizontal, right? It could be anywhere depending on the chord a1a2. Alright, those are three excellent exercises that will get you really appreciating the geometry of the parabola, and I hope starting to appreciate the fact that we're doing mathematics and we're doing calculus, and even though our origins were very elementary, it seemed as if we did almost no work, and suddenly calculus popped up in front of us. This should be disorienting to mathematics educators—if you're a mathematics educator out there, have a think about this, have a good long think about it. Next time, we're going to go up a step to talking about cubics. I'm going to apply our reasoning about tangents and derivatives to study cubic equations, which are a fabulous world of all kinds of interesting things about cubics. We're going to make some contact with that. I'm going to show you the prettiest theorem that I know in calculus, and you will not have seen it before, so it will be a real treat. Alright, so next time, the prettiest theorem in calculus and cubics. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening.

# 075 - Cubics and the prettiest theorem in calculus ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 75 [DAHBgcDJQjw].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. Today, we're going to look at the prettiest theorem in calculus, which is a theorem about cubic curves or cubic polynomials. This is one of a series of foundational lectures in mathematics where I'm trying to establish proper logical foundations for the subject. In the last few videos, we've been talking about calculus in a very elementary setting—the setting of polynomials. It turns out that we can do calculus this way purely algebraically, without any notions of limits, without any notions of real numbers, and with complete precision of thought. We're now going to reap the rewards by studying this beautiful theorem. So, cubics or cubic polynomials are something like this: as a poly number, it's just a list of four numbers. The four numbers are the coefficients of the polynomial when it's written this way in standard alpha form. So, this polynomial is of degree three if this coefficient, d, is not equal to zero, and we'll assume that from now on. Then it's called a cubic polynomial. Now, when we graph such a polynomial, in other words, what we do is we plug in points of the form, say, x-coordinate r and y-coordinate P(r), where P(r) is what you get when you replace alpha here with the number r. If you plot all those points, then you get a curve that looks something like this, perhaps sort of like this, or maybe going up and down like this, or maybe like this. So now, the study of these cubic polynomials and their graphs—essentially, we're talking about what are usually called functions of this kind—is part of a larger study of cubic curves, where we consider a more general kind of equations involving, say, x and y, polynomial equations where the total degree is three. These cubic curves were studied by Descartes, by Newton—they were of interest to almost all classical mathematicians because there's a lot of really interesting things that turn out to happen for cubic curves. Today, we're going to talk about a very special kind of cubic curve—these cubic polynomials. So, it's important to realize that there's a much bigger, more general theory, of which what I'm going to say to you now is only a small part. So, our strategy is to review calculus in this elementary setting, in the context of cubic polynomials, so you'll see exactly the steps that we need to go through to establish the foundations of calculus for cubic polynomials, and then we'll be able to state and prove this most pretty theorem. In fact, we'll be able to do that all on one page. So, our strategy is to first of all establish some of the basic calculus constructions for cubic polynomials. We're going to start with the general cubic polynomial a + b alpha + c alpha^2 + d alpha^3, and we're going to compute various things for it. We're going to compute the Taylor polynomial, or Taylor by polynomial; we're going to compute the derivative and various higher sub-derivatives, and then we're going to find the tangent polynomials or tangent poly numbers for this thing. Geometrically, that means we're going to be studying a curve something like this. Perhaps this is the particular polynomial 5 - 3x - 4x^2 + x^3, and then, say, at the point r = 1, the tangent line ends up looking something like this, and the tangent conic is a parabola looking something like this. So, we want to be able to compute for every value of r what the tangent line looks like, what the tangent conic looks like, and our theorem is going to be something about the tangent conics. Alright, so the first order of business is to find the Taylor polynomial for P. That, by definition, is just P(alpha + beta). We replace the alphas with alpha + beta, and then we expand that using the binomial theorem here and here, and then we collect terms—the terms not involving beta, the terms involving beta, the terms involving beta squared, and the term just involving beta cubed. So, it's just a question of expanding these binomial things out and putting it all together. Now, these three coefficients that are appearing here—actually, there's four of them including that one—these are the first sub-derivatives of the polynomial we started with. So, this thing here is D0 of P, this one is D1 of P or the derivative of P, this thing is D2 of P, and this constant D is D3 of P. D1 of P is just the same as the derivative, so this is the derivative of that. That's how we define what the derivative is—the derivative is the coefficient of beta in the Taylor polynomial. But at the same time, we also define the second sub-derivative, the third sub-derivative, and, in fact, the zero sub-derivative, which of course is just the same as the polynomial itself. So, just for good reference, let's write down these various sub-derivatives that we computed. The zeroth one was the polynomial itself; there's the first sub-derivative, or just the derivative; there's the second sub-derivative; and there's the third sub-derivative. Now, for those of you who are not used to sub-derivatives and just think in terms of derivatives, what is the second sub-derivative in ordinary language? It's the derivative of the derivative divided by two. So, if you take the ordinary derivative of this and divide by two, you get the second sub-derivative. And what's the third sub-derivative? It's the derivative of the second sub-derivative divided by three. Take the derivative of this, divide by three, and you get the third sub-derivative. So, if you just want to use differentiation, that's how you can go from one to the next—that's a simplified way of thinking about what the various derivatives are. Alright, now let's look at the Taylor expansion at r. Now, this is obtained by doing two things to the previous expression. The first is to interchange the roles of alpha and beta, so instead of expanding in powers of beta, we're now expanding in powers of alpha. We started with P(alpha + beta)—that's symmetrical in alpha and beta, so it's completely the same if we interchange the alphas and betas. And then the second thing that we do is that the betas, which are now appearing in each one of these things, are replaced with a number r. So, we get that expression: that's D0 of P evaluated at r, here's D1 of P evaluated at r, here's D2 of P evaluated at r, and D3 of P evaluated at r. Now we make a shift or a translation in this formula. We now replace alpha with alpha - r. So, translate, then the left-hand side becomes just P(alpha)—in other words, the original polynomial—and then the alphas, which are appearing here, here, and here, are replaced by alpha - r—there, there, and there. And this is called a Taylor expansion of the polynomial P at r because it depends on the number r that we've chosen. There's a different such expansion for every r, and this is a fundamental object because it's from this that we read off the various tangents to the polynomial. The first, well, the zero tangent, is just the value of the polynomial at r. If you set alpha equal to r, then these terms all disappear, and you're just left with that. The first tangent is the sum of the first terms all the way up to degree 1. The second tangent is the sum of all the terms up to degree 2. The third tangent is the sum of all the terms up to degree 3, which in this case is all of it. That's the general pattern of how we write down the tangents to the polynomial at r—we just take initial parts of the Taylor expansion. In other words, we truncate the Taylor expansion suitably. So, let's have a look at what the tangent line and the tangent conic simplify to when we look at them carefully. So, here are the first two terms in the Taylor expansion, the degree 1 part of it. What we want to do now is rewrite it. In other words, we want to expand it out and separate it into the things involving alpha and the things not involving alpha. So, here are the things not involving alpha to begin with, and then we're going to have to add to that minus r times each of these. And have a look at what happens when we take minus r times each of these: minus r times b will exactly cancel with this br that's already there. So, we'll have an a, but we will have no brs left. Here, there are cr^2, and we're going to add to that -2cr for a total of -cr^2. Here we have dr^3, and we're adding to that -3dr^3 for a total of -2dr^3. The coefficient of alpha is just this, so it's just copied unchanged. That is the tangent line to P at r, and here's our symbol for it: it's the first tangent, the first tangent to P at r. Okay, now we're going to look at the second tangent to P at r. We take the terms up to degree 2 in the Taylor expansion. So, the first two terms here are just the ones we've already obtained, and we've already simplified that, so why not just take what we've already got—the formula for the tangent line—and replace it there? That's what this plus this is, so we only need to add this now to it. That's c + 3dr, and here I'm expanding this out: alpha^2 - 2r alpha + r^2. Now, something pleasant happens—it's a little bit surprising that the thing becomes relatively simple; there's a lot of cancellation going on here—there's a little bit of magic trickling through. The constant term is only a + dr^3; for example, this minus cr^2 term that was there is going to be removed when we have an r^2 times c here, so those two combine to get rid of that one. The minus 2dr^3 that we have there, well, that has to be combined with 3dr^3 there for a total of dr^3. What about the coefficient of alpha? Well, here's how many alphas we have to start with, and then we have to add a certain number of alphas here. How many? Well, from here, -2cr alpha—that's exactly canceling this one—and also -6dr^2 times alpha—we already have 3dr^2 times alpha, so we're going to have -3dr^2 altogether. And finally, the coefficient of alpha^2 is the same as it is here; we just copy this thing over. This is a polynomial of degree 2—it's called a tangent conic to P at r. There's a polynomial of degree 1—that's the tangent line to P at r; this is the tangent conic to P at r. And it's all just high school algebra. So, I've just copied what we've already determined here: there's the original polynomial, there's the tangent line to it at r, there's the tangent conic to it at r. So, for example, if the polynomial happens to be the one that we drew a picture of earlier, and if we're looking at the point r = 1, then if you take this expression here, substitute a = 5, b = -3, and so on, and then plug in r = 1, you can check that you get 7 - 8 alpha—that's a line. And correspondingly, if you plug the various values into here, you get 6 - 6 alpha - alpha^2—that's a parabola opening downwards. And you can check if you go back to that previous picture that these are more or less the line and the tangent conic that I drew earlier. Let's make a nice little observation. We'll observe that if we're looking at the tangent line, its slope is just the derivative of P evaluated at r. That's the same thing that we found for quadratic polynomials—that the slope of the tangent line is the first derivative evaluated at the point in question. We see here that it's also true for cubics—in fact, it's true for any polynomial. The other observation is that the coefficient of alpha^2 in the tangent conic—so, the corresponding top term here—that also has an interpretation in terms of a sub-derivative: that's the second sub-derivative of P evaluated at r. So, in the formulas for the tangent line and the tangent conic, these terms are already dictated by the first and the second derivatives. These three terms here are perhaps a little bit less predictable but make the subject sort of interesting. So, that finishes our preliminaries having to do with calculus. Now I just want to say a few words about a special cubic—the simplest cubic, P equals alpha^3. There's a graph of it; it flattens out around zero, of course, it increases rather dramatically as you keep going, and it has three important properties that it's worth pointing out. First of all, it's an odd function, meaning that if you evaluate it at -r, you get -P(r). So, the value at -2, for example, which is -8, is related to the value at 2 by being negatives—8 and -8 are negatives. That's obvious because -r^3 is the same as -(r^3). The second observation is that P is increasing. If r1 is less than r2, then when you evaluate P(r1), it's less than P(r2). That's perhaps clear if you just look at the right-hand side first. Okay, if you have two numbers, r1 and r2, and r1 is less than r2, well then, if you multiply r1 by itself three times, you're going to get something less than if you multiply r2 by itself three times. So, it's increasing on this side, and then by symmetry, or the odd property, it's also increasing on this side—in other words, as we go right, it goes up. And the third observation is that this is what's called a one-to-one polynomial. What that means is that if the polynomial values at two points agree—in other words, if P(r1) = P(r2)—then in fact r1 = r2, which is to say that the function never takes on the same value at different points. So, this is often referred to in terms of a horizontal line—if you have a horizontal line, then it may or may not meet the graph as it turns out, but if it does meet the graph, it only meets it at one point, right? It's not the case that there's a point there and another point there on the graph—the polynomial is one-to-one. Okay, so with these preliminaries, here is the prettiest theorem in calculus as far as I'm concerned. Let's see if you agree. So, we'll give this theorem a name: call it the Cubic Disjoint Tangent Conic Theorem. It's a rather long-winded name, but it pretty well tells you what the theorem says. It says that the tangent conics of a cubic polynomial are all distinct, and in more detail, that means there's no point (x, y) which lies on more than one tangent conic, so tangent conics never meet. We have a tangent conic at every point on the curve, so for every point on the curve, there's a parabola, or perhaps sometimes a line in a degenerate case, but typically a parabola opening either downwards or opening upwards. So, if we plot a lot of those quadratic polynomials, the tangents at a whole number of different points, we're going to find that there's no overlap whatsoever between any of those quadratics that we've drawn. No matter if we draw a billion of them, none of them overlap. Okay, here's a proof. The proof is short and sweet. Suppose P is, as before, our usual polynomial a + b alpha + c alpha^2 + d alpha^3 with d not equal to zero, and suppose that we have some point (x, y) lying on some tangent conic associated with the point r. Well, here is the equation for the tangent conic, and if (x, y) lies on it, it means that y = this quantity plus this quantity times x plus this quantity times x^2. This is, after all, the equation of that tangent conic that we wrote down before, so it's all contained in this equation here. Let's have a good look at it. So, what we're going to do is we're going to put all the terms involving r on that side and all the other terms on this side. So, there's a y, the a comes to the other side as -a, the bx comes over here, the cx^2 comes over here, and this term—I'll explain in a minute—on the other side, we have dr^3. In fact, all these three terms on our left will have a factor of d, so we'll pull that out, then we'll have an r^3, a -3r^2x, a +3rx^2, because we pulled the d out front. And at this point, we recognize, or should recognize, this is almost a binomial expansion of (r - x)^3, and to make it such an expansion, we add -(r - x)^3 inside this bracket. So, we've added -d(x^3) to this side, and to compensate to keep things equal, we're going to add -d(x^3) inside here. Well, then this is just d(r - x)^3. And what's that telling us? It's telling us that the value of r for which the tangent conic passes through (x, y) is given by this equation. This is some number, d is some number which is nonzero, so something equals (r - x)^3. But we just saw that the polynomial alpha^3 was one-to-one, so there's at most one r that can possibly have this property. There's at most one value of r such that this thing cubed equals this number divided by d. That means there's at most one tangent conic that goes through the point (x, y)—you can't find two tangent conics going through (x, y), only one. The r is given by this equation, and that determines r completely. And that's the proof of the theorem—it means that through every point, there's at most one tangent conic going through that point. The surprising nature of this result becomes clearer when we look at an example, especially when we try to draw some of these tangent conics. So, here is that cubic that we were looking at before—the one with the equation 5 - 3 alpha - 4 alpha^2 + alpha^3. And here is a formula for the tangent conic of this thing at r. So, I'm replacing the a, b, c, and d with 5, -3, -4, and 1, and that's the formula that we get. Now, for various values of r, you can sub in here, and you're going to get different conics appearing, and I've graphed some of them at least roughly here for you. Probably the most interesting and most important one is actually the one that occurs right here at the point 4/3. 4/3 is an important point for this cubic—in fact, it's the most important point on the cubic—it's the inflection point. That's exactly the point at which this second derivative is zero, so at which point the tangent conic becomes a degree one curve instead of being a degree two curve. So, it means that for that particular value of r, this gives a line, and it's in fact that line there in green. So, that's the tangent conic at 4/3, or the second tangent at 4/3—it's not really a conic, it's a sort of degenerate conic. This is sort of a line of inflection of the curve. Now, here are some other tangent conics: at the point 2, the tangent conic looks like this, roughly. At 3, the tangent conic is this one, roughly. At 4, it's like this. At 5, it should be something like that. As we go on further, we get more and more parabolas in that region there. On the other side of the line of inflection, say at 1, which is just a little bit to the left of the inflection point, the tangent conic there is pretty close to being a line—locally, it looks like that. Of course, it's part of a big parabola. Then at zero, the tangent conic looks like this—parabola opening downwards. At -1, it's like this. At -2, it's like this. And as we keep on going down, there are more and more parabolas inside here. So pretty well every point in the plane is approximately on one of these tangent conics—the tangent conics fill up the visible plane, at least approximately. And if you actually draw a more accurate diagram, it's a bit of a marvel to me that all of these curves can fit together in such a beautiful way—they're all tangent to the original cubic, all exactly tangent at different points, but they all miss each other just dramatically, and they all sort of snuggle together to form kind of a foliation, like a bunch of leaves or lines that fill up the plane but are curved. Alright, so as far as I know, this is a new theorem. It's a little bit surprising to me that it's new—it's surprising that it's not well-known, and I very well doubt that it's really new. It's impossible for me to imagine that 19th-century mathematicians would not have discovered this at some point. But there are so many works that have been lost in libraries and in dusty tomes that maybe someday we'll have access to electronically, but currently, they're still essentially buried to us. So, it's a little bit hard to say for sure that something is new, but I think this theorem really catches one's eye and deserves attention. This is just a start to cubic curves, and I think it's a good place to introduce an important new family of objects. We're going to talk now a little bit about algebraic curves, with the idea of extending our idea of conics and cubics from this polynomial point of view to a two-variable point of view. So, this is going to allow us to deal with much more general curves, and the question of how does one do calculus with more general curves is a very interesting one, especially how do we do elementary calculus like we've been doing here? Do the techniques extend? If so, how? We're going to make a start on that next time when we have an introduction to algebraic curves. I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music] 

---

# 076 - An introduction to algebraic curves ｜ Arithmetic and Geometry Math Foundations 76 ｜ N J Wildberger [3qwAZe7ni7w].en-en

Kind: captions Language: en [Music] Good day, I'm Norman Wildberger. In this video, we're going to have an introduction to a very big subject—the subject of what is a curve and, more particularly, what is an algebraic curve. So, we're going to start to investigate notions that we're going to have to say a lot more about in upcoming videos. I'm going to show you some pictures, we're going to have a little bit of historical discussion, and I want you to start thinking about curves and the question: How should we approach what a curve is? It's historically a very interesting and problematic notion. The motivation is that in the last half a dozen videos or so, we've introduced what we might call algebraic calculus. This is an approach to calculus that is really oriented at a high school student—it's very elementary, it doesn't involve any notions of limits, of real numbers, or of functions. For us, so far, this algebraic calculus has been developed in the context of polynomials, and it allows us to define and utilize Taylor expansions, sub-derivatives, and to talk about tangents. Tangents mean not just tangent lines but also tangent conics and higher tangents. A natural question that many of you probably have is: Okay, that's good, that's interesting, but how are we going to extend this algebraic calculus to more general functions? For example, those of you who have studied some mathematics before, you've seen functions perhaps like this: 𝑥 2 − 1 𝑥 4 + 3 x 2 − x 4 +3 1 ​ . You can think of that as a function of 𝑥 x according to the standard dogma, or you could think of the square root of 𝑥 3 + 1 x 3 +1, or maybe 𝑥 x to the fifth, or perhaps the exponential function combined with a circular function, 𝑒 tan ⁡ ( 𝑥 ) e tan(x) . So, does this algebraic approach to calculus work for these kinds of things? If so, how? Some of you might also be wondering how we're going to extend this elementary calculus to go beyond functions to consider more general curves—for example, conics or cubics. If you are interested in an ellipse, for example, an ellipse like this, perhaps you know that in the standard formulation of things, an ellipse like this has an equation involving both 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y, not just 𝑦 y equals a function of 𝑥 x, but a relation involving both 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y together. So, that's not really a function because for any value of 𝑥 x, there's more than one associated value of 𝑦 y. But nevertheless, it's a reasonable geometrical object. We might very well want to know, for example, how to compute a tangent and how to compute the slope of that tangent at some point. Here's another example: 𝑥 2 16 − 𝑦 2 25 = 1 16 x 2 ​ − 25 y 2 ​ =1. That's a hyperbola. It's also not a function in the usual sense, but it's certainly a pleasant geometrical object. I've also shown you a curve that is a little bit more complicated—this one here in red that has the equation 𝑥 3 + 𝑦 3 + 3 𝑥 𝑦 = 0 x 3 +y 3 +3xy=0. It's called the folium of Descartes. That's an example of a cubic curve, and again, we might ask not just how to calculate a tangent, but how do you calculate a tangent conic at a point? And here's another one—this one right here—that's given by an equation like this, which is actually of degree four. So, this is another question: Can we calculate Taylor expansions of these things? Does it make sense to talk about sub-derivatives? Can we compute tangents, for example, tangent lines, tangent conics, or maybe even tangent cubics for higher-degree curves? Alright, so what we're really asking here is: Is there such a notion of algebraic calculus on an algebraic curve? The idea being that these things should be algebraic curves because ultimately they are given by polynomials involving 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y. So, they're reasonably pleasant algebraic objects—there should be a pleasant calculus for them too. Of course, that brings up the question: Well, what exactly are we talking about here when we talk about an algebraic curve? What does that mean? So, let's have a historical look at these questions by going back to the ancient Greeks to start with and having a look at what they were thinking about in the context of curves. Greek mathematics emphasizes the importance of the straightedge and compass for making geometrical constructions and, correspondingly, the notions of line and circle as being the fundamental curves or mathematical objects out of which most other things are built. So, those are the primary curves in Greek mathematics. Then, sort of one level up, they discovered the conic sections. The conic sections are obtained by essentially combining the circle and the line to get a cone. The cone is what you get when you take a circle and then a point not in the plane of that circle and draw all the lines from that point to the various points on the circle—you get a cone. And then the conic sections are obtained by slicing that cone with a plane. If you slice the cone with a plane in that orientation, you get an elliptical cross-section. If you slice it with a plane something like this, then you cut both the top and the bottom parts of the cone to get a hyperbolic cross-section, or hyperbola. And then, sort of halfway between these, poised between them, is the case when the plane is just parallel to one of the lines of the cone, so it cuts the cone only in one of its parts, and that way you get a parabola. So, these are the three standard kinds of conic sections, but there are, in fact, degenerate ones as well. We could also consider the case when the plane slices right through this point here in such a way that it also, say, slices through those two lines there, forming an "X." So, a pair of intersecting lines is also a conic section. In fact, if we took a plane that went like this, then we would be just picking up one line, so one line—one might think of it as a doubled line—is also a conic section. And the most degenerate case is when you take a plane that just slices the cone at its vertex there—then you're only picking up a single point. So, this notion of conic sections does have degenerate cases too. These were well studied by Archimedes and Apollonius prominently, and the ancient Greeks learned a lot about them. But they also considered quite a few other curves, some of them very interesting. For example, Archimedes introduced the idea of a spiral, and he thought very mechanically, physically. He imagined a line that was fixed at a point rotating uniformly about that point—actually, sort of a ray, I guess—and then imagined on that line a particle moving linearly along that line at the same time that the ray is rotating. Okay, so if the particle starts there, then after a little while it's here, and then it's there, sort of trying to follow it roughly something like this. So you get this spiral shape, which goes on indefinitely, and he proved some very nice things about it. These days it's most easily described in polar coordinates using the radius and theta and has a relatively simple equation in terms of those. There's the cissoid of Diocles, which is obtained from a circle, and here are two perpendicular diameters of the circle. From this point here, we go an equal arc in this direction and in this direction. So, once we've gone equal amounts in this direction and this direction, we take the line perpendicular to this diameter through that point, and then we connect this point with that point there to obtain the intersection right there. As we change these two points, making them wider or further apart, then this intersection traces out a curve. If we consider the corresponding pair up here, we also get this part of the curve. The curve is naturally extended—it really wants to keep going, even though the construction doesn't do that for us, but it wants to keep going. It turns out that algebraically it has an equation like this: If you put the origin at the center of the circle, 𝑦 2 ( 𝑎 + 𝑥 ) = ( 𝑎 − 𝑥 ) 3 y 2 (a+x)=(a−x) 3 , where 𝑎 a is the radius of the circle. Then there was the conchoid of Nicomedes, which was also around 200 BC, and it's also something that's reasonably natural. It's also formed by lines, so we have a fixed line there, we have a fixed point here, and then we're considering the various lines going through our fixed point, and we're looking at going a fixed length from the baseline on any one of these lines. So, on any one of these lines, we then go a fixed length in the direction from the line, okay, and then these points trace out some curve, which will sort of asymptotically approach the line as we go out in that direction, and that's the conchoid of Nicomedes. I think he actually made a mechanical construction that would actually draw this thing for you. So, the ancient Greeks did think in terms of mechanical constructions for their curves. Another kind of curve that was considered are the spheric sections, where you get a torus, and a torus, or a donut, is what you get when you take a circle and you rotate it around an axis. So, this has a circular cross-section, and this is also a circular direction here, so we're rotating a circle around a circle to get a donut. And then these spheric sections are the curves that you get by slicing this donut with a plane, and the various curves that you get depend very much on where the plane is in relation to the donut. You can get a pair of circles, or you get kind of an oval shape, or a curve that kind of indents a little bit, or a very interesting sort of special case is when the curve is such that it crosses there, and you get a sort of figure-8 curve. Of course, ancient peoples were very interested in astronomy, and in particular, figuring out what's going on with the planets in the night sky. To understand that, Ptolemy introduced the notion of an epicycle, which is a curve that's formed when one circle rotates about another circle, and we're watching the image of a point. So, if this point rotates, if the circle rotates, then the trajectory of such a point is something like this, looping around the bigger circle. Alright, so the ancient Greeks had a variety of curves that they were interested in, and they had both a mechanical and geometrical understanding of these curves. The next really important development is in the time of the Europeans around the 17th century with René Descartes and his fundamental treatise La Géométrie, in which he introduces the idea of algebraic coordinates to deal with geometrical problems. It's from this that we get our notion of Cartesian coordinates, with an x-axis, a y-axis, equally spaced labels along each axis, and the idea that you can represent a curve by an equation, and conversely, given an equation, you can picture it by a curve. An absolutely fundamental innovation and advance in mathematics—probably in pure mathematics, perhaps one of the most important innovations in the last 500 years. Descartes realized that the power of this point of view was that the algebra tells you a lot about the curve, and you can calculate purely algebraically instead of having to make geometrical constructions. He realized that if you took the simplest possible relation between 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y, namely a linear relation of the form 𝑎 𝑥 + 𝑏 𝑦 + 𝑐 = 0 ax+by+c=0, then you're getting a line, and conversely, a line always has a linear relation like this. And he would have been very excited when he discovered that if you go up one level to degree 2 curves—so you're considering all equations of the form 𝑎 𝑥 2 + 𝑏 𝑥 𝑦 + 𝑐 𝑦 2 + 𝑑 𝑥 + 𝑒 𝑦 + 𝑓 = 0 ax 2 +bxy+cy 2 +dx+ey+f=0—and here these numbers 𝑎 , 𝑏 , 𝑐 , 𝑑 , 𝑒 , a,b,c,d,e, and 𝑓 f are all numbers. So if you consider that kind of equation, then the corresponding point ( 𝑥 , 𝑦 ) (x,y) traces out a conic, and every conic can be expressed in this form. So, this is an incredibly exciting development—he could then see how to prove pretty much everything that Apollonius knew about conics by working with the algebraic representation of them. He also saw that there's a natural hierarchy of curves depending on the degree—that's the linear degree 1, that's quadratic degree 2—so there's a natural progression of complexity of curves depending on the degree. One can't underestimate the importance of this development. So, I think anybody who's mathematically minded when they hear about this story naturally they're going to ask, well, alright, if degree 2 curves are the conic sections of the ancient Greeks, what are the degree 3 curves? Absolutely natural, obvious question—what do they look like? Well, people were interested, of course, and Isaac Newton, famous for many things, of course including the development of the calculus and the laws of motion, including resolution of the conundrum of how the planets move, investigated cubic curves with the idea of taking a general cubic curve, which rather has this long, intimidating structure. So it's a combination of 𝑥 3 , 𝑥 2 𝑦 , 𝑥 𝑦 2 , 𝑦 3 x 3 ,x 2 y,xy 2 ,y 3 , and then lower degree terms like 𝑥 2 , 𝑥 𝑦 , 𝑦 2 , 𝑥 x 2 ,xy,y 2 ,x and 𝑦 y, and just a constant. So, he took one of those equations and said, well, if I graph that, what am I going to get? What does it look like? And he made various transformations to try to get them into sort of standard forms. Just as there are more or less three conics—at least three non-degenerate conics—he tried to analyze how many cubics there were, and I think his list has some 70 or 80 in it. It wasn't completely correct, but anyway, the cubic story is much more involved than the conic story, but still very interesting. So, here's just some brief indication of what cubics can look like. So, in black here, that's a possible shape of a cubic. See, it actually has two components, or it could look like this thing here, or it could look like this with sort of a cusp, or it could look like a bunch of branches, maybe even with an isolated point, or it could have even three branches like this. There's quite a lot of possibilities for what a cubic looks like, and of course, then there's the corresponding question: Well, what about the higher degree curves? Once we've understood cubics more or less, what about quartics—fourth-degree curves—or quintics—fifth-degree curves—and so on? And I have to say that, of course, nowadays, this is subsumed in algebraic geometry, but it's a bit of a mystery to me why algebraic geometers don't spend more time and energy explaining to the rest of us what the answers to this natural question are. I think it'd be lovely to have more pictures of algebraic curves so we can see some of the variety and the beauty of these objects. Actually, there's a reason why they don't do that, partially, but we'll get to that later when we talk more about algebraic geometry. But you can see here that, okay, these kinds of curves that result from thinking about the degree of a polynomial in 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y in a Cartesian setting introduced by Descartes—very reasonable, very natural, perhaps very pleasant field of investigation. But there's a lot more to curves than these things. So, the 17th and 18th centuries saw a lot of work and development of not just the algebraic curves that we've been talking about, but also other kinds of curves that had often a mechanical formulation. For example, a catenary, which is the shape formed by a hanging chain. So, you get a chain and you hang it from two equally spaced points—you get some kind of curve, and it's natural to ask, what is the shape of that curve? People thought maybe originally it was a parabola, and it's maybe reasonably close to a parabola, but it's actually a different curve called a catenary. Another important curve that's related to motion is what happens when you roll a circle on a line—it's just like Ptolemy's epicycles except that the rolling circle is rolling on a line instead of a bigger circle. So, if we choose some point on the circle and we follow its path as our given circle rolls, it's going to form something that looks like this—that's called a cycloid. And that cycloid had a lot of remarkable properties—it was not an algebraic curve, but people eventually realized that it had a lot to do with the laws of motion. So, if you invert a cycloid—in other words, just turn it upside down—instead of having a branch like this, we have a branch like this, then if we think of the motion of a particle, like a ball rolling down that curve, this has two very interesting properties. First of all, it's what's called the tautochrone, meaning equal time curve. And what that means is that if you start the ball anywhere along this curve, anywhere from here up to here, and you drop it and let it roll down—assuming there's no friction—then the amount of time that it takes to get there is fixed. So, it takes the same amount of time to roll down from the top as it does from here or even from here. It's a remarkable property of the inverted cycloid. And then another property is the brachistochrone property—that's actually the shortest time curve. If you want to slide down a slide from this point to this point and you consider all possible curves between these two points, including, for example, a straight line, which is an obvious or a very primitive kind of slide, then it turns out that exactly this inverted cycloid is the curve that lets the ball go down fastest—anything else that you choose will be slightly or much slower. And the names associated with these results are Huygens and Jacob Bernoulli, Jacob being one of the many Bernoulli brothers that played an important role in mathematics. Alright, so the Europeans were very fascinated by curves, and they had both an algebraic and a mechanical orientation to them. But even going back to the founder, to Descartes, Descartes realized that one should make a distinction—that there was something special about the algebraic curves that were given by polynomials. And he sort of had this division into mechanical and geometrical—these days people say algebraic versus transcendental, perhaps, but it's more or less the same thing. So, the idea that the algebraic curves given by polynomials are in some sense more amenable to analysis and investigation than these transcendental curves. But still, it begs the question: What exactly are we talking about here when we're talking about a curve? So, it's a rather subtle question of what exactly is a curve, and historically, it's caused quite a lot of problems, and there's been a lot of debate and a lot of introspection and analysis of figuring out what exactly we are talking about when we're talking about a curve. It's easy enough to put our pen down on paper and squiggle around a little bit and get something and say, well, that's a curve. But what exactly are we doing? How are we mathematically modeling that? What is allowed, and what is not allowed? It's rather surprising that the modern definition only really took place in around the last part of the 19th century. In the 1880s, Jordan gave this following modern definition of a curve. He said that a curve is a set of points taken on by a pair of continuous functions 𝑥 = 𝑓 ( 𝑡 ) , 𝑦 = 𝑔 ( 𝑡 ) x=f(t),y=g(t) where 𝑡 t ranges through some interval on the real line. Okay, I put "continuous function" here in quotes—he would not have put it in quotes, but I do because the notion of a continuous function is, in fact, highly problematic, as we'll see later on. But to Jordan, he thought he knew what a continuous function was, and so he thought, well, this is what it is—it's just a pair of continuous functions specifying the 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y coordinate of a point on the plane. Okay, this is a very unfortunate definition—it's a completely wrong idea, it's very wrong thinking, and it's far too simplistic, and it's logically just cheating, as we'll see. But there was already evidence shortly after Jordan gave his definition that there was something fishy going on here. Just a few years afterward, in 1890, Peano gave an example where a curve, which is a curve by Jordan's definition, had a remarkable, in fact almost absurd, quality. So, he showed that you could cook up two continuous functions, 𝑓 ( 𝑡 ) f(t) and 𝑔 ( 𝑡 ) g(t), which purportedly took the interval from 0 to 1 onto the square in such a way that it was one-to-one and onto and continuous. That means that there's actually a bijection, so this curve, which is not like this—this is some kind of simplistic rendition of it—but this curve, whatever it is, takes every point here to every point here in a one-to-one way, so that the curve as the set of points obtained is actually the unit square. So, according to Jordan's definition, the unit square is a curve. Okay, so most people, when looking at this example, would say, alright, well that just proves that the definition is wrong. If I have a definition of a circle, for example, and we ultimately decide that a lemniscate is a circle according to my definition, then the natural deduction is that my definition is not a very good one—that we should forget about it and try something else. That would have been the natural deduction to make from Peano's example. And then other people gave other examples, including Hilbert. But convenience took precedence over logical clarity. These days, the definition of a curve is more or less this one, except that people have changed their tune just slightly to say, okay, the curve is not actually the image, it's actually the pair of functions, so the pair of functions is the curve. But still, Peano's curve is still a curve in the modern definition. Students are usually not shown this in an introductory calculus class because it's a little bit embarrassing, just as it was embarrassing in the last part of the 19th century. A lot of embarrassing things happened in the last part of the 19th century, by the way, okay? Things that are still casting their shadow over modern mathematics—we're going to talk a lot more about them. So, if the notion of a curve is perhaps at this point too ambitious, perhaps we should scale down our intentions and try to do something more modest. Why don't we stick with algebraic curves just the way Descartes urged us to do? That's much more amenable, much more treatable with logical clarity, and there's a natural way forward going back to Descartes and Newton and the pioneers of the subject. So, an excellent way of starting to think about curves is by using the framework of bipolynomials. Let me remind you that a bipolynomial is a two-dimensional version of a polynomial—it's a rectangular array of numbers, and most of the time we'll be using examples where the numbers are integers. So, here, for example, 1, 2, 0, 3, -1, 4, 3. And if you like, you can think of zeros as occupying these other points; I'm just too lazy to write them down. And this has an alternate expression in terms of alphas and betas, so roughly oriented the same way here. This is 1 + 2 𝛽 + 0 𝛽 2 + 3 𝛽 3 − 𝛼 + 4 𝛼 𝛽 + 3 𝛼 2 1+2β+0β 2 +3β 3 −α+4αβ+3α 2 , so increasing powers of 𝛼 α in this direction and betas in this direction. We would write this more usually just in a linear fashion: 1 + 2 𝛽 + 3 𝛽 3 − 𝛼 + 4 𝛼 𝛽 + 3 𝛼 2 1+2β+3β 3 −α+4αβ+3α 2 . So, whether we're talking about this as a polynomial in alpha and beta or this thing as a bipolynomial, it's pretty much the same thing. Now, to connect it to the discussion we've just been having, we can replace alpha with 𝑥 x and beta with 𝑦 y, for example, to get an equation. So, associated with this, we can say, oh, we can just think about this equation—this equation is somehow intimately connected with this bipolynomial. So, some examples: The folium of Descartes, which is a cubic, has this equation—it can be associated with the bipolynomial or bipolynomial right here. So, this represents the 𝑥 3 x 3 , this represents the 𝑦 3 y 3 , this represents the 3 𝑥 𝑦 3xy as a bipolynomial. It's 𝛼 3 + 𝛽 3 + 3 𝛼 𝛽 α 3 +β 3 +3αβ. So, in other words, whether we're studying this thing or whether we're studying this thing, maybe they're pretty close, maybe they're somehow connected—maybe we can think about what this is as being this. So, here are two other examples: the lemniscate of Bernoulli, which is this sort of figure-8 shaped thing here, has this traditional equation ( 𝑥 2 + 𝑦 2 ) 2 = 2 ( 𝑥 2 − 𝑦 2 ) (x 2 +y 2 ) 2 =2(x 2 −y 2 ), and what that means is that the points that are lying on here are exactly the points that satisfy that equation. Well, we can expand that out if we want to and bring everything to one side, then we get an expression of degree four in 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y, and if we prefer to use alphas and betas, we can replace 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y with alphas and betas, and then we can say, aha, what we've got here is just a bipolynomial—it looks like this. There's a 1 for the 𝛼 4 α 4 , one for the 𝛽 4 β 4 , here's the 2 𝛼 2 𝛽 2 2α 2 β 2 term, the minus 2 𝛼 2 𝛽 2 2α 2 β 2 , and the plus 2 𝛽 2 𝛼 2 2β 2 α 2 . Here's another example: the Fermat curve, 𝑥 3 + 𝑦 3 = 1 x 3 +y 3 =1. Well, if we bring everything to the other side, it's sort of like 𝛼 3 + 𝛽 3 − 1 α 3 +β 3 −1, which can be represented by a bipolynomial like this, and its graph looks something like this. Now, there are all kinds of subtleties involved here. This is just an introductory talk—we haven't actually yet pinned any proper definitions down. I just want to get some examples out there on the table—these are the kinds of objects that we would like to consider. Now, there's all kinds of subtleties, however. For example, it's a famous theorem of Fermat that this particular curve actually only has two rational points on it—the only pairs of numbers 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y which are rational numbers are these ones here that lie on the curve: (1, 0), that's there, and (0, 1). So, what am I actually drawing when I draw this? That's a good question. Modern mathematicians will say, oh, I'm drawing real numbers—I'm drawing all the non-rational solutions of this thing, the irrational solutions of this, all properly in quotes. Well, that's highly debatable, and we're going to talk about that. But the same question arises here—can we actually find points that lie on this equation? It's one thing to write down an equation and say, alright, we're going to look at all those points ( 𝑥 , 𝑦 ) (x,y) that satisfy the equation, but how do you actually go about finding some solutions? You see, this is very different from the situation when we were graphing polynomials. There, we had a polynomial 𝑃 P, and we just graphed all the points ( 𝑥 , 𝑃 ( 𝑥 ) ) (x,P(x)), so we are guaranteed to get points because we start with 𝑥 x and then we plug 𝑥 x into 𝑃 P and we get the 𝑦 y coordinate. This is much different because the 𝑥 x and 𝑦 y are intermixed here, and we don't have them in the form 𝑦 = y= something, so you can't immediately generate solutions to this. It's highly non-obvious, and it's highly difficult in many examples to actually come up with points that actually lie on these supposed curves. Okay, so you can see some of the complicated issues that are related to this notion. But this is a course in foundations of mathematics, and so these kinds of issues are bread and butter for us—we're going to want to examine this in great detail. We want to get this right. Most of modern mathematics tries to hurry-scurry its way past these foundational issues—quick, quick, quick, do it very fast, then we'll talk about something else. Not us. We're interested in going very slowly, very carefully, making sure we understand what exactly is going on. Next time, I'm going to introduce an important idea that will be necessary for us to really understand what a curve is. I'm going to talk about the difference between 20th-century mathematics and 21st-century mathematics. 20th-century mathematics is behind us; 21st-century mathematics is largely in the future. There's going to be a big difference, and I'm going to talk about an aspect of what I believe that big difference is—the difference between object-oriented mathematics and expression-oriented mathematics. Then we're going to see that that's quite pertinent to this question of how exactly do we go about setting out what a curve is, what an algebraic curve is, and how we do calculus with such things. So, I hope you'll join me for that. I'm Norman Wildberger. Thanks for listening. [Music]
